The War Office this morning attempted
to explain the summary of the situation
In Natal, furnished by the commander in
chief of the forces. Field Marshal Lord
Wolseley, to the House of Commons yes
terday. His statement Is now said to
have created unnecessary alarm and it is
asserted that what the official note called
General Yule's "retirement" would bo
more accurately described as a "change
of position for tactical reasons," and that
the alarm for the safety of the wounded
is not justified. It is further explained
that the wounded could not be moved,
but the fact that they had to be left to
'their fate while the British forces .made a
strategic movement to the rear shows the
suspense of the House at the absence of
subsequent news is amply justified and
that the heavy fighting on Friday and
perhaps Saturday is perhaps a prelude to
still sterner work. In some quarters the
view is taken that the second attack of
the Boers has actually occurred and was
so successful that General Yule's with
drawal was imperative and urgent to the
extent of compelling him to leave his hos
pitals in the hands of the Boers, while
the more sanguine express the opinion
that General Yule, far from retreating,
has in reality advanced to meet the at
tack threatening Glencoe, leaving Dundee
behind, safely sheltered from a Boer
assault.
In the absence o{ authentic news, there
are a number of contradictory rumors
afloat, but the most reasonable interpreta
tion of the various reports seems to indi
cate that there was some artillery prac
tice at Glencoe Saturday and Sunday, but
no fighting at close quarters.
As, however, communication between
Glencoe and Ladysmlth, which was
thought to have been restored by the Brit
ish victory at Elands Laagte, has again
been broken by the destruction of the
railway bridge at Waschbank, north of
Ladysmlth, and between Elands Laagte
and GJencoe, the difficulties of the British
commander- in Natal, General Sir George
Stewart White, in reinforcing Glencoe
have been enormously Increased, and un
THE BATTLE OF ELANDS LAAGTE.
til the British forces at Ladysmith and
Glencoe are reunited and the main body
of the Boers is brought up, the situation
must remain disquieting for the British.
The afternoon papers here sharply criti
cize Lord Wolseley's summary of the
Natal situation. They say it has a dis
tinct resemblance to the statements of the
Spanish Ministry when preparing their
countrymen for news of the disaster at
Santiago. This, however, is probably an
overstrained view. There is no denying,
however, the great suspense and anxiety
existing, which has been increased by the
report in circulation, purporting to eman
ate from official quarters, to the effect
that the Boers have secured the services
of 13,000 natives.
The vague and varied estimates of Boer
losses and the absence of any official in
formation on the subject are also arous
ing misgiving's as to whether the beaten
enemy suffered proportionately to the dis
astrous losses of the victors. One of the
most disquieting stories comes from Lady
smith. It is to the effect that an English
man, who arrived there from Dundee on
Sunday evening, after escaping through
the Boer lines on the previous night, said
the enemy was then shelling the camp
and town with heavy guns, while the
shells of the British were unable to reach
the enemy's batteries. Consequently, the
man is said to have added, the camp was
shifted a mile or so. In order to be out
of the reach of the Boers, who were firing
on the magazine in the town.
News has at length been received direct
from Colonel Baden-Powell, dated Mafe
king, October 15. It confirms the state
ment that In the armored train fight the
British had two men killed and fifteen
wounded, including- Lieutenant Lord
Charles Benedict of the Ninth Lancers,
slightly wounded. The Boer loss was es
timated at fifty-three killed and many
wounded.
The latest advices from Kimberley, un
der date of October 21, said the Boer at
tack was still pending, and that large
Boer forces in the neighborhood had de
stroyed big sections of the railroad line
north and south of the town. Many fu
gitives from neighboring villages have
been imprisoned by the Boers. Several
ladles, hearing their husbands had been
captured, visited the Boer camp and were

berlej.
Dispatch riders who are coming down
to the Orange River from Kimberley are
performing thrilling feats. They ride
through the Boer lines under cover of
darkness and will get to Orange River
without taking any rest, save for change
of horses. The distance is sixty miles.
One rider who rode Into Kimberley last
Friday was chased for seventeen miles
by the Boors, narrowly escaping with his
life. This same man returned safely with
dispatches to Orange River to-day. When
ho had reached a point thirty miles from
Kimberley his horse fell and kicked two
lingers off the . m's hand against a rock,
but despite his painful wound 1 he gallant
fellow made good the journey here. He
report? that the Boers are three miles
from Kimberley, but are afraid to attack
and are waiting for the arrival of Cronje.

OVERTURES FROM BOERS
LONDON, Oct. 24.â The Colonial Office
this evening published a dispatch re
ceived from the British High Commis

from Sir Godfrey Laigden, the British

Basutos, whom, he says, he has been try

adds:
"Our policy, however, has been marie
difficult by the blustering of the Boers,
who have freely threatened to attack
Masuru and other stations. These threats,

tributed to rouse excitement among the
natives. I wish to place it on record that
the Bqeri have unwisely attempted t i
shake the allegiance of the Basutos and
frustrate our efforts toward tranrjuility.
; The BoerS", therefore, are responsible for
any commotion and for the alarm regard
ing native Invasions which now prevail."

GERMANY'S STAND
AS TO INTERVENTION
BERLIN, Oct. 24. -The Tageblatt denies
the statement of the Eclair of Paris that
Germany after promising assistance to
France and Russia for the purpose of in
tervention in the Transvaal seems now
to withdraw it. The Tageblatt declares:
"We believe we are well Informed when
we state that the German Government
did not participate in any combination for
Intervention."

CHICAGO, Oct. 24.â Forty two wives scattered throughout the world, four

charged with bigamy. Farnsworth also admitted that he was a man of many
aliases. Some of these are Charles Bradford, A. J. Hitting, S. L. Thomas, A.
L. Kiefer and Bradshaw.
"I cannot tell exactly how many women I have married," he said. "I
know of eleven In Europe, four in China, thrse in Peru, one in England and
over twenty in different parts of the world, hut, to save my soul, I couldn't
tell how many. I married th-m for different reasons. I did not live more
than a day or two at a time with them. They will tell you I was good to
them."

TRIAL OF THE COEUR
D'ALENE MINERS DELAYED
It Is Now Very Probable the Hearing
Will Not Commence Before
Next Monday.
MOSCOW, Idaho, Oct. 24.â 1t now seems
probable that the trial of the Coeur
d'Alene miners on a charge of interfering
with a United States mail train will not
begin before Monday next. The United
States Grand Jury is now in session here,
and it is probable that the new indict
ment it will present against the men will
Include the nances of tho?e in the old In
dictment and also the names of the "four
hundred whose names are unknown."
Out of the 442 Indicted by a former
grand jury, It is not probable that more
than twenty will be tried. These will, it
is thought, be tried jointly. The rest of
the indicted miners are out of the State.
have been paroled or have escaped. The
defense will ask the court to pay the ex
penses of their witnesses as they are un
able to do so.
Edwin Brooks, a defaulting witness be
fore the Grand Jury, was brought in to
day and heavily fined.
EXPEDITION RETURNS.
Failure of the Shushitna River Ex-
ploration Party.
PORT TOWNSBND, Wash., Oct. 24 â
The United States Government expedi
tion In charge of Captain Glenn which
went north to explore Shushitna Rh-er
has returned to Valdes to winter. It was
the Intention of the expedition to winter
on the Shushitna River, but owing to the
failure of the arrival of supplies in time
to take them up the river, the expedition
was compelled to return to Valdes, where
supplies were landed by the steamer
Cleveland.

DRAMATIC SUICIDE
OF GEORGE PLANT
Sitting Beside His WifÂ« on a Bale of
Hay in His Bam, Shoots Him
self With a Shotgun.
SAN DIEOO, Oct. 24.â George Plant,
who shot himself with a shotgun last
nighi while sitting in his barn on a bale
of hay beside his wife of only two months,
died this morning at 3 o'clock, without
explaining his rash act. His was a deter
mined* case of suicide, at; he had quietly
drawn oh* one shoe, and, taking up his
6taolgun, had asked his wife to move over
a little. Nol suspecting his intent she did
so, as she thought she had hidden all his
gun cartridges. Plant then pulled the
trigger with his toe and the charge en
tered his side. He tore, oft bandages when
placed on the wound by the doctor, say
ing he was determined to die.

CATTLE HERDS
ARE STARVING
Caught by the Heavy
Snowfall.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN ANDREAS. Oct. 24.â More than
one hundred experienced vaqueros are
working day and night trying to save four
thousand or more cattle that were caught
on the ranges of the higher Sierras by the
unexpectedly severe storms. The snow
has destroyed all feed and unless the cat
tle can be brought out they will purely
starve The cattle men of Tuolumne
County are greater sufferers than those of
Calaveras, many bands owned here hav
ing been brought down last month, but
these aggregate less than five hundred
head.
It cleared up yesterday, however, and
the worst if past, for the present. The
rainfall here for the present storm was
nearly five Inches and the snowfall In the
upper ranges was from six to eight feet.
Wliile it has brought death to the cattle,
and even deer, it will prove a blessing
to a wide scope ,of country next summer.
The cold weather which was a feature of
the great storm solidified the snow, caus
ing it to pack hard, and thus insure a
general supply of water next year.
Though the sun is shining brightly to-day
the air is cold and crisp and the snow will
not melt.
Some sawmill men had narrow escapes
and Surveyor McTarnahan of Angels was
snowed in above Strawberry Valley, in
Tui.lumne County, but was rescued by a
relief party.
The homo and family of Assemblyman
Harvey B, Blood of this county, on the
divide between <"alaveras and Alpine
counties, are Bnowed in, and the local
statesman Is prepared f'>r a hard siege.
All work at ponlatowkt's Blue Lake
City in doubling the capne'ty of the Stan ].
ard electric plant has come to a standstill
and the men have all come out. All work
in the timber belt and at the sawmills has
been suspended for the season.
CATHOLIC CHURCH
CONTROVERSY ENDED
Excommunication Removed From the
Troubled East St. Louis
Parisli.
ROME, Oct. 24.- The following is the
full text cf the official instructions from
Cardinal Iyedochowekl to the Bishop of
Belleville, 111., bearing on the recent con
troversy in the Catholic church of East
St. Louis:
"Remove immediately the excommuni
cation from the parish, as they do not
deHerve it. Get Father Cruze to resign
and appoint a pastor of their own
nationality over them."
Cardinal I^edochowski is Cardinal prefect
of the Propaganda, to whom belongs the
final settlement of all ecclesiastical con
troversies of this nature. There is no ap
peal from his ruling. It follows on these
instructions that the general practice of
the church for the future duties will be
that Bishops must appoint pastors ac
cording to the nationality of congrega
tions.

SALINAS, Oct. 24.â One of the prettiest
wedding ceremonies as well as most
notable society events in Monterey
County occurred to-night, when
Frank W. Sargent and Miss Mary Wat
son were united in marriage. The event
took place at the country home of the
bride's parents. Corral de Tlerra. Rev.
Father Stoeters officiated.
Miss Watson is the daughter of Mr. and
Mrs. Thomas Watson, both of whom are
natives of California and are children of
one of the pure Castillan families who
came to this State in the forties. Miss
Watson is a native daughter. Her pa
rents have lived In Monterey County for
a great many years and have become" very
wealthy.
Frank W. Sargent has been Deputy
County Clerk since 1897. Mr. Sargent is
a native son. He is a graduate of Santa
Clara College, where in 1896 he took the
degree of A. B. He studied law until May'
1897, since which time he has been in poli
tics. Mr. Sargent is first vice president
of the parlor of Native Sons in this citr
and president of the Young Men's Insti
tute.
MANY VICTIMS OF
KENTUCKY FEUDS
Crimes in the Blue Grass
Region.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LONDON*. Ky., Oct. 24.â Tom Witmore
and Dan Parker were ambushed yester
day on Horse Creek, en route to Clay
County Circuit Court. The former was
killed and the latter injured. Bob Travis
was killed at Hamlet.
A report states that a Philpot, en route
to Manchester Court, was ambushed and
killed at Pigeon Roost to-day.
Trouble is reported on Sexton's Creek,
between the "White and Baker factions.
Circuit Judge Kversole. fearing assassina
tion, did not go to Manchester and the
opening of the court was delayed. The
Griffins and Philpots are present in large
numbers, heavily armed. Manchester is
crowded and the situation looks grave.

MADE MORE RIGID
Manila Papers Shall Publish Noth=
ing Regarding the Movement
of Troops.
MANILA, Oct. 24.â General orders Just
Issuer prohibit publication in the public
press here of any item of news reporting
directly or indirectly the arrival or de
parture or transfer or other movements
of troops. It permits the publication of
reports of battles or of movements com
pleted. The object of the order is to stop
the loc.nl custom of anticipating the move
ments of troops, supplies or ammunition.
Heretofore the papers were allowed to
print everything, while the cable dis
patches to American newspaper* were
sometimes detained.

TRAIN BLOWN BY GALE
FROM A HIGH BRIDGE
Heavy Loss of Life Attends Disasters Caused
bu a Terrific Tijptioon In
Japan,
VICTORIA, B. C, Oct. 24.â News was
brought by the steamer Rio Jun
Mam, which arrived this evening
from the Orient, of the ravages of
a terrific typhoon, said to have
been the worst ever experienced at
Tokio and Yokohama, which visited those
cities and the Japanese coast on Satur
day, October 7 and the day following.
One of the disastrous consequences was a
shocking railway accident, which oc
curred on the River Hokigawa, on the
Japan Railway Company's line.
Just as the storm was developing its
greatest force a train happened to be
crossing the iron bridge over the Hoki
gawa. This bridge, which is situated be
tween the stations of Yalta and Nozaki,
has a length of 1044 feet. The river,
though of small dimensions under ordi
nary circumstances, had been consider
ably swollen by the rains. The train,
from its exposed position on the bridge,
could not resist the pressure of the wind.
The locomotive, owing to its greater
weight, crossed safely, but all the car
riages were tumbled into the bed of the
stream, their coupling with the locomo
tive being severed. The loss of life is
estimated at between thirty and forty
and twenty-three persons were severely
wounded. Fourteen bodies have been
taken out of the wreckage of the train in
the river.
On the Toklado line there was a tidal
wave flooding the whole district and in
the Tago-No-Tra region a wall of water
twenty-five feet high swept up over the
land, "carrying death and destruction. It
NO STRIKE ON
VALLEY ROAD
Slight Grievances That
Will Be Settled.
Special TMspatch to The Call.
STOCKTON, Oct. 24.â For some days
past rumors have been In circulation that
a strike on the Valley Railroad was im
minent. A large number of employes
were interviewed to-day and without ex
ception they declared that no such prop
osition was under consideration. The
men could not account for the rumors.
However, there la some dissatisfaction
among the men. especially in the me
cbanical department, over the fact that
the Santa Fe has failed to extend its
wage schedule to this recently acquired
division. â .
"There is no ground for a strike in
which we could secure the aid of other
trainmen," said an engineer to-day. "So
long as a road is in course of construc
tion the genÂ« rul schedule cannot be de
manded. We have been working under
what is known as the construction
schedule. When the Point Richmond
branch is opened ami put in operation 1
doubt not that the general schedule of
- inta Fe. will be extended here. 1 am
speaking now of the trainmen or actual
operatives of the rolling stock. Even if
we had a grievance there would be no
strike The days of strikes on railroads
arc over. If we treat the company fairly
we will come out all right."
The force In the machine shops has
cut down one-half and It is
understood that the muchinery is to be
removed to Albuquerque and only enough
left hefe to do emergency repairs. As
many of the men had been working for
two "years in the hope of advancement,
the decision of the company came to them
;,s Lad luck and left them in no very good
humor. As a matter of fact there are
only about twenty regular engineers, con
ductors and liremen at present in the em
ploy of the company, and should they
Wave their places could be promptly
filled. Many of the employes have every
reason to be loyal to the company and not
<4iiibble over any decision the company
may make. Several of them had be<n
out of employ since the big strike and
\v.>re takm on regardless of how they
might have been marked on the old com
pany's lists. I'ndoubtedly there is dis
satisfaction among some of the men. but
the idea of a strike Is laughed at. There
have been some agitators and one or two
men quit last payday in a huff, but even
though the situation might become
strained the men say they would send a
committee to headquarters and do not
doubt that any differences would be ad
lusted satisfactorily to all concerned.

and Shinhama. Fifty-three persons were
killed, thirty-six severely Injured and 150
slightly injured.
The wave piled up such a quantity of
sand at the mouth of the Xuma River
that the river was dammed and over
flowed its banks, producing an inundation
of large dimensions. Numbers of houses
were swept away and all that is to be
seen of those remaining is the roofs.
In Tokio and vicinity the damage was
extensive and a number of lives were lost.
Along the coast hundreds of houses were
destroyed and many craft lost. At Oiso
and Koiso the storm was felt with ex

land. At Oiso the steamer t'mezawara

An Informal luncheon. In honor of Sir
Thomas Upton, owner of the Shamrock,
w;is given to-day in the rooms of the
Transportation Club In the Manhattan
Hold, a number of railway and steam
boat transportation officers were present.
Senator Depew, president of the club, re
ceived the guest. The Senator in an ad
dress said:
No man has contributed so much to the
speed of England and bo much to exhilarate
this country a? Sir Thomas. He wanted that
cup. We stopped twisting the lion's tail a
year ago and this year we had to stick pins
in the eai^le. The result has been satisfac
tory to tfco eagle.
It is the sporting: spirit in its best sense
that carries Kngland's flag and the spirit '>f
Knglaml over the world, and it is the sport
ins spirit in Its best sense that carries our
flag to the other side of the world. The same

three or four days ago, when we received

I have been asked If I would challenge
afrain Unfortunately Mr. Fife, the designer

now.
Now, gentlemen, I am prepared to make an
other challenge when Mr. Fife regains his
health and I can discuss the matter with

Less Damage Than Expected.
WOODLAND. Oct. 24.â The clear, cold
weather and prevailing north wind are
minimizing the damage to the raisin crop
and the wheat in sacks piled along the
river and railroad tracks for shipment.
Unless there should be another storm
soon the damage will not be so heavy as
at nrst estimated.
phoon, It sustained considerable damage.
While the storm lasted the force of the
wind was terrific and the scene in the
harbor, especially outside the defense
works, was remarkable. Great seas rolled
over the breakwater. The French mail
steamer Tonkin collided with the Ham
burg-American steamer Serbia, both be
ing- damaged, and havoc was played with
the yachts anchored in the harbor. The
town was flooded, the water being in
many places kneedecp.
The storm had Its origin where many
storms have been bornâ in the Riukla
Islands. The depression declared itself
first on the 4th inst., but showed a dispo
sition to pass into the Pacific, and hopes
were entertained that Japan might thu.s
escape without any serious visitation. On
the 6th, however, the center began to
move in a northeasterly direction and it
was then evident that these islands would
be invaded. That night the center reached
Oshima, and on the seventh the storm
swept into the southeast corner of Kiu
shu. Thenceforth it coasted along the
east of Kiushu and Shikoku, the center
probably following a maritime course
and only the skirt of the storm being felt
on shore. But the curve of the main
island brought it nearer the path of the
depression's center, and when the ty
phoon, after crossing from Shikoku,
struck the shore again in the neighbor
hood of Hamada, the winds were exceed
ingly violent. All along the eastern coast
the effects must have been felt very se
verely.
IS SHOT DEAD
DURING A ROW
T. D. Perry of Liberty,
Or., Killed.
BpÂ»cial Dispatch to The Call.
SAL.EM, Or., Oct. 24.-Joseph A. Wil
lard proved himself a good marksman to
day when he killed T. D. Perry at Perry's
farm near Liberty, four miles south of
Salem. The killing was the result of a
row over a lease.
An examination to-night by the physi
cians showed that four of the five bullets
fired by Wlllard entered Perry's body,
three of them in vital places. One en
tered Perry's heart, another punctured
both lungs," the third passed through sev
eral intestines and the fourth lodged in
the left arm.
Willard rented Perry's farm and a con
troversy had existed between them as to
the time the lease expired. Perry going
so far as to threaten to kill Willard. Both
men met at the farm to-day and after a
few words Perry reached into his hip
pocket as if about to draw a gun. Wil
lard fired without hesitation, and as a
result Perry now lies on a slab at the
morgue.
After the shooting Willard gave him
sf-lt" up to Sheriff Durbin and is now in
jail. Both men were residents of Salem.
Perry, who is about 55 years old, left a
widow and two children.
Coroner Clough will hold an inquest to
morrow.

HEART DISEASE.
Some Facts Regarding the Rapid In-
crease of Heart Trouble.
Heart trouble, at least among the Amer-
icans, is certainly increasing, and while
this may be largely due to the excite-
ment and worry of American business
life. It is more often the result of weak
stomachs, of poor digestion.
Real organic disease is incurable; but
not one case in a hundred of heart trou-
ble Is organic.
The close relation between heart trouble
and poor digestion is because both organs
are controlled by the same great nerves â
the. Sympathetic and Pneumogastric.
In another way^ also the heart is af-
fected by the form of pf>or digestion,
which causes gas and fermentation from
half digested food. There is a feeling of
oppression and heaviness in the chest
caused by pressure of the distended sto-
mach on the heart and lungs, interfering
with their action; hence arises palpitation
and short breath.
Poor digestion also poisons the blood,
making it thin and watery, which irri-
tates and weakens the heart.
The most sensible treatment for heart
trouble Is to Improve the digestion and
to Insure the prompt assimilation of food.
This can be done by the regular use
after meals of some safe, pfeasant and
effective digestive preparation, like
Stuart's Dyspepsia Tablets, which may
be found at most drug stores, and which
contain valuable, harmless digestive ele-
ments in a pleasant, com'enient form.
It is safe to say that the regular per-
sistent use of Stuart's Dyspepsia Tablets
at mealtime will cure any form of sto-
mach trouble except cancer of the sto-
mach.

by druggists at 50 cents. Little book on
stomach troubles mailed free. Address F.

much the same. George Lamhy, through ;

HORSE THIEF SHOT
BY PURSUING OFFICER
As the Criminal Falls He Attempts
to Swallow Poison From a
Bottle He Carried.
SPOKANE, Wash., Oct. 24.â Marshall
Dethridge of Oakesdale to-day shot in
the back and probably fatally wounded
a horsethief he was pursuing. As the
robber fell he grabbed a bottle 1 of poison
from an inner pocket and swallowed a
portion of the contents before he could
be restrained. The officer hurried the
man to the nearest physician. Emetics
were given and other means taken to pre
vent fatal effects from the poison. The
bullet wound, however, is expected to
prove fatal, although the robber is still
alive.
The Marshal was acting under tele
phoned instructions from Colfax that a
thief had stolen a team and buggy there.
The man was discovered at the outskirts
of town. He refused to surrender and
attempted to escape when the Marshal
nred. The bullet penetrated the back
near the t spine and low down. The wound
ed man refuses to give his name. He de
stroyed all identity, even eating the
leaves of a book in his pocket that might
identify him. In the buggy there was
found a couple of saddles and bridles, and
two sets of harness evidently stolen. The
Sheriff is expected here this evening and
if able to be moved he will take the pris
oner to Colfax.

Nome guaranteed them plenty of good
provisions on the way down. Vhey wero
charged <50 for their passage.. When they
were a few days out the meat, sugar and
butter ran out. Their meals consisted of
salt horse and a small supply of canned
mutton. There was a little dried fruit on
board, but only enough to supply the
table three times with dried peaches and
twice with dried prunes. From ihat time
on, for nearly twenty-four days, their
food consisted of flour and coffee. There
was plenty of flour, but the water â was
short. Four days ago the last of the un
palatable salt horse was consumed. Had.
it not been for rainstorms, which fell for
a few days, they would have been with
out water also.
When the Hera anchored out In the
stream the majority of the men were so
weak that they could not carry tneir gold
dust ashore without assistance. Several
passengers were crazed from their terrible
experiences, and had to be carried ashore
and taken care of. Even taking their
terrible voyage into consideration, the
passengers unite in declaring Nome to be
the greatest camp on earth, and many of
them will return in the spring.
BREASTWORK ABOUT
THE TOWN OF NACO
] Mexican Authorities Preparing to
Resist Threatened Attack by the
Cowboys.
BISBEE, Ariz., Oct. 24.â Intense excite
ment has reigned among both American
and Mexican residents at Naco all day to-
I day. The Mexican garrison was rein
! forced late last night by detachments of
infantry and cavalry from La Marita and
i Magdalena. As soon as the additional
; forces arrived the Mexicans began pre
paring for an attack. They threw up
! breastworks around the town, extending
i to within 300 feet of the boundary line.
' Several field pieces were also rolled into
; position and the works were manned by
; a cordon of soldiers with an advance line
j of mounted pickets thrown out to patrol
i the border and keep a lookout for the in
vaders.
Babe Thompson, the recognized leader
;of the cowboys, has counseled them
, against making an assault on the Mcxl-
I can works until every means has been
: exhausted to secure the release of the
two prisoners without resort to violence.
Judge Mendoza held court last night
I and sent word across the border that he
i had decided to release Joseph Rhodes on
| a bond of $S(XiO. The bond was promptly
1 made and Rhodes was surrendered to his
j friends. George Maarts is still being held
, and the indications now are that he will
i also be released within a few hours.
SUICIDE BY HANGING.
Mrs. Susan Bitchley Ends Her Life
at the Napa Asylum.
NAPA, Oct. 24.â Mrs. Susan Ritchley,
a native of California, aged 39 years, and
an inmate of the Napa State Hospital,
committed suicide by hanging In the bath
room of her ward at noon to-day. She
was committed to the institution June 11.
1899. from Humboldt County, where her
relatives reside. Her husband resides in.
San Francisco.

Buchanan was suspended for his effort
astride Rainier. According to the state
ment of Dr. O'Rourke, the horse's owner,

make any effort to <]<> so. On the con

The Western Turf Association's first
list of kes, which will be run during
November and December, will close to
day. The management of the new racing
venture announce that the track will open
on November 4.

arrived yesterday and will '..â¢â¢ seen in the
saddle to-day. Morgan was the sensa
tion of the East last spring, until he got
.-: hard fall while riding Squire Abingdon,
suffering a compound fracture of the
shoulder. On more than one occasion he
nearly caused Spencer to fall, off his
mount, beating that well-known rider in
several desperate finishes. Morgan has
Cherokee blood in his veins, and it Is un
necessary to a?k if he is trying.

Beer Killers Fined.
The State Fish and Game Commission
ers have long recognized I^ake County as
ono of the worst localities In California
In the matter of violations of the game
laws. Men there have killed deer and
feathered game without regard to the
Ftatutes or seasons, and recently a move
was made to bring some of the hunters
to justice. The close season for the pro
:. of deer began on the ISth in-st.. and
Deputy Kercheval was Bent to the.
\y Valley district. He was not there
long before he caught three hungers with
the evidence of guilt In their possession,
they having Just nhot two deer. The
prisoners were Ben Woodruff. T. F. Burke
;u;<i Ike Kerr. The fellows pleaded guilty
hofore Justice Thompson and paid fines
of $20 each.

TROOPS TO BE WITHDRAWN.
Military Forces Will Soon Retire
From Their Post at Wardner.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 24.-Governor
Steunenberg of Idaho had another confer,
enco with the Secretary of War this after
noon in regard to the withdrawal of the
Federal troops in Idaho. Governor Steun
enberg has protested very earnestly
against the withdrawal, but Secretary
Root haa named November 1 as the lat
est date at which the troops will do guard
duty over the riot prisoners. It 1b not an
nounced that the troops will be with
drawn altogether from Idaho or from the
Coeur d'Alene country on that date, but
It is not likely that they will bo kept away
from their home posts much longer.

Golden Gate Park Driving
Association Abandons Its
Ingleside Meetng.
The members of Golden Gate P.irk
I>rivins Association met last nighi and
after extended discussion determined to
abandon their autumn race meeting for
which they had planned ambitiously. All
arrangements had been perfected to hold
ii last Saturday, but th>- heavy m.ins left
the track deep with mud. over which
hors<-s could not draw the racinu' sulkies.
Tho conditions were such it waa thought
best to declare the meeting off altogether.
The track would requin a lot <>f attention
to place it in proper condition, while some
of the owners wished to withdraw their
horses from competition. To please those
who still wished to race, It was decided
to shift the scene of operations from
Ingleslde to the I'ark speedway.
A programme of four races will be run
off there on Saturday, November 4 for
trophi>s valued at $20, t<> which wiil be
added the entranci teva of $â¢"> per b 1
Each race will be best two in thrfc beats
over a distance of three-fourths of a mile.

mit "f satisfactory races at one mile.
Entries will close on the 31st |nst., and
all tin- horses now in training are expect
ed to compete.
Tho race for the Frank G. O'Kane cup
will not b>- Included in the programme, as
the conditions require that it be raced
for on a circular track. Efforts will prob
ably be made to retain the double team
race, as that Is one of tho most exciting
of contests. Amateur drivers ulftrm that
more double teams have been in evidence
In the Park during the past fortnight than
in many years previously.
George W. Kingsbury was elected a
member of the association at last night's
meeting.

his black horse I. Direct and Dunlap's
King Cadenza, a mile in 2 :30, over Ingle
slde track yesterday. The going was par
ticularly heavy, which makes the per

COMTE SAW SOME STARS
WHEN GODEAU HIT HIM
RECITED HIS RECOLLECTIONS
OF RECENT TROUBLE.
Witnesses Summoned on Behalf of
His Assailant Testified That
He Was Angry at the
Ball Committee.
Tf the testimony of the many witnesses
Who were on the stand In Judge Dainger
field's court yesterday Is to be accepted
it Is certain that never again will such
'â xi'itomont I><^ known at a meeting- of the
Ligue d'Henrl IV as that which prevailed
â¢ luring that momentous occasion upon
which M. Osmln I'nmte collided with the
Drazen fist of M. Jules Qodeau, under
taker.
The trial of the suit instituted by Comte
to recover a small furtune in damage*
from Godeau for the pain and Inconve
nience he Buttered through the alleged
fracture of his rmsc and the lÂ°>ss of the
sight of one of his ryes from the blow
delivered by the defendant was con
tinued yesterday morning. Mr. Comte was
called to the stand to further impress
upon the jury the way in which he was
assaulted, and this he did, saying:
"Qodeau he strike me so fast I don't.
know what with. I remember when they
?aek me out. I did not have my senses.
only see stars and light." So it went
on through the day.
Charles F. McCarthy, P. Alex Bergerot,
Jean Mefllette, James Masson and P. Pel
lison were called by the plaintiff and all
gave evidence as to how Godeau. when
Comte objected to the motion to award
the ball committee a vote of thanks,
walked over and overruled the objection
by punching the objector into the land
of dreams. Henry G. Gerdes, John A roes,
P. Cediey and A. Mlgueu were called on
behalf of the defendant and all corrobo
rated the defendant's contention that
Comte, angered at being denied the bar
privileges at the ball, went to the meet
ing with the express intentinn of making
trouble. He made trouble and found it,
they said. "Comte was jus strike at by
Godeau," said one witness. At this
Comte turned his twisted proboscis for
the inspection of the jury to show them
the effects of the blow, and the case went
over till this morning.
A Sick Fund Benefit.
A special benefit performance for the
sick fund of Buena Vista Parlor No. 63,
Native Daughters of the Golden West,
will take place this evening at the Tivoll
Opera-house, the bill being Offenbach's
comic opera, "La Belle Helene." The
management of the Tivoll has joined
heartily in the plan to raise funds for the
worthy cause and the artists of the thea
ter will do their best tn-nlsht to make the
affair a memorable one. A larg-e number
of seats hnve been disposed of by the
ladies of Buen^Vista Parlor and a crowd
ed house is anticipated.

JEFFORDS
LOSES IN
THE FIFTH
Californian Makes a Good
Showing Against Gus
Ruhlin.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. Oct. 24.â Jim Jeffords, the
young California pugilist, made his debut at
the new Broadway Athletic Club to-night
and was knocked out in the fifth round by
Gus Ruhlin, the Akron (Ohio) giant.
There was a lot of slugging in the first
four rounds, and if Jeffords had not mixed
things so freely he might have stalled off
Ruhltn's rushes. The latter simply bat
tered him down after Jeffords had gained
a very decided advantage. Ruhlin '.
him three times in the fourth round ami
put him out with a straight Jeft early in
the next round. Ruhlin was a big favor
ite in the betting, 2 to 1 being laid -a him.
In the preliminary bout Kid Thomas of
New York won from Jim H^yward (col
ored) of Chicago in the fourth round.
Thin was Hayward's lirst defeat since
coming East. lie was knocked down
three times in the fourth round.
Gus Rulilin was the tirst of the big
fellows to enter the ring. He looked tit.
He was accompanied by his manager and
trainer, Billy Madden, and "Cockey "
Boyle, Charley Goff and Joe Hurst, who
acter as his seconds.
Ten minutes later Jeffords appeared.
He had as his seconds "Mysterious" Billy
Smith, Billy Needham, Charley O'Brien
and Harry Luthill. Jeffords said he
weighed 192 pounds and Ruhlin owned up
to 190. They were announced I
twenty rounds, under Marquis of Queens
bery rules.
First roundâ rushed and Jeffords sent his I
'.eft to the body. They clinched. Jeffords sent i
his rlÃh to Ru'hlins car and then to the body.
Ruhlin clinched and In the breakaway sent his.
right to the body. , â
Second roundâ Jeffords rushed, trying left |
and right swings for the head, which were
blocked. Jim Jabbed left to body and face
and then shot his right to the jaw. Gus Jabbed
left to the face. Both began slugging, with j
Jeffords having the call up to the bell.
Third roundâ Jeffords rushed and Gus ewung
wildly. They exchanged leftg on the face and
then Qua whipped right and left to the head
and smashed a hard right on Jeffords" nose,
which began to bleed. Jeffords staggered from
this blow and Ruhlin slugged him. The Call
fornlan looked to be beaten, but he suddenly
braced up and sent a couple of right upper
cuts under Ruhlln's chin, which changed the !
tide in his favor. Ruhlin was very much used j
up at the close of the round.
Fourth roundâ They got to close Quarters
three times In succession. Jeffords was the ag
gressor. Hard exchanges followed, among
them being a right uppercut from Jeffords,
which sent Gus back a few feet. Ruhlin pulled
himself together quickly and sent three straight
lefts to the face and sent Jeffords to the floor
with a short right-arm Jolt on the Jaw. Jef
forda took eight seconds to get up, only to be
sent down again with a straight left. Jef- j
fords managed to get to his feet and hugged :
Ruhlin when he got up, but Gup shoved him
off and beat him down with left-hand chops.
Jeffords was floored for the third time and was
practically out when the gong sounded.
Fifth round â Ruhlin now had it all his own
way and went right nt his man. He led a left I
to the face, which staggered Jeffords to the;
ropes. Then he sent both hands swinging to
the head, and as Jeffords staggered, this
stepped hack and, putting all the force of his i
big frame into the blow, he landed a straight ,
left hand squarely on the mark and Jeffords
fell as if dead. He lay prone on the floor
until the referee counted him out Time of
round, one minute and fourteen seconds.

Rough Rider Married.
William Hentzler, one of "Teddy" Rons
velt's famous regiment of Rough Riders,
was married yesterday to M rs. Hope Silyy
of this city. Justice of the Peace Kerri
gan performed the ceremony. Hentzler
has been out of the service only nine
months, and came on here a few days
ago to cast his lot with some regiment
leaving for the Philippines. He expects
to sail to-day.

THE SAM FRANCISCO CALL, AVEDXESDAY, OCTOBEK 'J5. ISO 9.
WILL UNITE THEIR
HEARTS AND FORTUNES
MISS MADGE CASSIDY.
AN exceedingly pretty -wedding will be performed at noon to-day
at St. Mary's Cathedral, when Edward Joseph Deasy, son of Super
visor Jeremiah Deasy, and Miss Madge Cassldy, daughter of Mrs. M.
J. Cassldy, will be Joined In the holy bonds of wedlock by Rev. Father
Philip O'Ryan. The youth of the contracting parties, Mr. Deasy
being but 23 years of age and his bride several years his junior, and their
popularity among a large circle of friends, make the happy event a more than
usually interesting one.
The bride will be given away by Isidore Goulet. an Intimate friend of the
family, and she will be attended by her sister. Miss Minnie Cassidy, as brides
maid. The groom will have his brother. Daniel Deasy, for best man, and the
ushers will be Charles Drury and William Cathcart.
Immediately upon the entrance of the bridal procession Into the sacred
edifice Professor Harrison, organist of the church, will play the wedding
march from "Lohengrin," and just before the ceremony is performed S. J.
Sandy will sing "Salve Regina." After the nuptial knot Is tied the party
will be driven to the home of the bride's mother at 321 Golden Gate avenue,
where a sumptuous wedding breakfast will be awaiting them. The residence
has been beautifully decorated with smilax, evergreens and flowers of every
description expressly for the occasion, and presents a handsome appearance.
The names of the invited guests are: Mrs. ]'. Drury, Mrs. M. Bohanon,
Mrs. J. Fitzgerald, Mrs. James Marshall, Mr. and Mrs. Jeremiah Deasy,'
Cornelius Deasy, Mrs. John Quinn. Miss Sadie Quinn, Miss L. Bohanon. Miss
A. Shelley, Edward Torpey, W. D. Mulhern, James Carlton, Charles Drarv,
Daniel Deasy, Father O'Ryan and Isidore Goulet. The newly wedded couple
will leave on the afternoon train for Del Monte, where they will remain ivvo
weeks, and from there will proceed to Pacific Grove for a week's sojourn.
On their return to this city they will reside with the bride's mother.
Edward Deasy is a prominent member of the League of the Cross Cadets,
and two years ago won the diamond medal for the best essay on "Temper
ance." He is connected with the Palatine Insurance Company. His bride is a
recent graduate and is a great favorite In her large circle of acquaintances.
FUNSTON WILL
NOT ENTER INTO
A CONTROVERSY
Says Archbishop Is
Misinformed.
The answer of Archbishop Ohapellc to
ments concerning the Catholic
church in the Philippines, said to have
been made last week by Brigadier Gen
eral Funston in his address to the stu
dents at Stanford University, will prob
ably cl..se the incident. General Funston,
who had r.tired early last night. BOme
what the worse for the weather, sent
word to a <'all representative, who had
called at his home in Oakland, that he
diil not care to enter into any controversy
in the matter, and that Archbishop Oha
p. lie had undoubtedly been misinformed
concerning what he had said.
Th>- Archbishop, as will be seen by a
reference to his statement published in
yesterday's Call, takes the view that Gen
eral Funston. whom he characterizes as
a broad-minded and fair man, had not
been correctly quoted. In support of
this proposition the testimony of Pn-si-
Davld Starr Jordan is important.
Referring to Archbishop Chapelle s criti
cism of language said to have been used
by General Funston, he said last night:
"General Funston can speak for himself
as to his opinions and the basis on which
they rest. it is, however, only fair to
him to say that the language quoted is
so twisted, through condensation, that :1c
original force is lost. He said nothing to
the discredit of the Catholic church and
nothing as to 'confiscation of church
property,' save as to the possible secu
larization of lands held In trust by the
oni>T of Spanish friars. He said that
though the natives of T^uzon bitterly
hated the friars, they are nevertheless
devoted to the Catholic church."

THE TWENTIETH KANSAS
I Over 3000 people gathered In Mechanics'
Pavilion last night, the attraction being
the entertainment of the Twentieth Kan
sas Volunteer Regiment. The affair was

: army department of the Young Men' a

United States Volunteers;, Governor
Shaw and staff of lowa, Colonel J. C.

! and Brigadier General F. Funston.
I The programme opened with a band

lowa band (Julius G. Gomez, bandmaster)
i and the Twentieth Kansas United States
Volunteers band (E. C. Strickland, chief
musician), and at 9 o'clock the Kansas
i volunteers, under the command of Colonel
W. S. Metcalf, made their entry. At
! dress parade the whole audience joined
in singing "America," led by the bands,
and then came the march and counter
march in review.
When the regiment had completed Its
evolutions and was drawn up before the
reviewing stand Henry J. McCoy, secre
tary of the Young Men's Christian Asso
elation, Introduced Mayor Phelan, who
delivered a brief address, In which he
spoke of the high regard entertained by
the citizens of this community for the
! members of the Kansas Regiment.
The regiment was then dismissed and
! gathered in the dining-room, where an
informal reception to the officers and men
! took place. A collation was served by the
I mothers' club of the associationâ Mrs. O.
B Wright, chairman: Mrs. H. A. Hill.
vice chairman; Miss Florence T. Perkins,
secretary; Mrs. T. C. Pedlar, treasurer.
A promenade concert concluded the
| evening's festivities, the lowa's band and
i the Kansas band alternating in playing
1 the musical selections, and a number of
' the guests and soldiers availed themselves
i of the opportunity to indulge in dancing.

EDWARD JOSEPH DEASY.
H. P. LOVELANP, NEW MANAGER OF THE TRAFFIC LEAGUE.
CARTER FAILED
TO REMEMBER
HIS PROMISES
Estate Sued by Mary
E. Redmond.
By the filing nf an answer yesterday
issues have been joined in the suit insti
tuted by Mary E. Redmond against the
estate of the late Thomas Carter, in
which the plaintiff seeks to recover $10,000
damages for alleged breach of promise on
the part of the deceased capitalist. 'me
case will now go to trial, and the world
will hear the story of the amours of the
dead car-builder of N< waik.
In the complaint in this action the
plaintiff alleges that in June of 1596 she
instituted suit against Carter to recover
$50,000 damages for breach of promise.
Carter negotiated with her for a settle
ment and finally she agreed to accept $2000
and a promise that he would leave her
$10,000 by his will. Carter died, the plain
tiff was not mentioned as a devisee, and
hence she seeks to recover the sum named
from his estate.
In the answer filed yesterday Martin
Carter, executor of his brother's will,
after entering a general denial to the
various allegations of Miss Redmond, sets
forth in defense an alleged waiver of all
claims against the dead signed and issued
by the plaintiff. This document rf^ads:
Know all men by these presents: That I,
Mary E. Kedmond, for and in consideration of
the sum of $5400 and other valuable considera
tion to me in hand, paid this day by Thomas
Carter, the receipt whereof is hereby admitted
and acknowledged, have remised, released and
forever quitclaimed unto Thomas Carter from
all mimnÂ»r< ot actlona and demand? whatso
ever, lncl'iding any and all agreements and
promisen of marriage (which said agreements
and promises of marriage and to marry said
Thomas Carter hnth now and forever denied),
and any and all claims of, and for, support
aril maintenance, by prisons of uny Inter
course or association I may ever have had with
him or any other claims, and I further prom
ise and agree not to begin any suit against
Thomas Carter in any court for any matter
whatsoever, nor to consult nor engage any at
torney in that behalf.
It is said that as the date of the~in
strument precedes the date of tho filing
of the suit during Carter's life to recover
$50,000, it being dated July 10, 18M, It
will not stand as a valid defense in plain
tiff's action.
Sent Bad Meat to Town.
N. F. Witzeman, Chief Meat Inspector,
swore to a complaint in Judge Treadwell's
court yesterday for the arrest of "John
Doe" Vecchl of Vecehi Brothers, San
Rafael, on the charge of violating the
health ordinance by sending diseased
meat into the city. Policeman Butter
worth, who is attached to the Board
of Health, went to San Rafael yesterday
afternoon to make tho arrest. Simi
Brothers of this city were arrested for
having i.he meat in their possession, but
they proved to Judge Treadwell yester
day that they were innocent of its con
dition, and it was decided to arrest the
consignor.
For Suffering Cubans.
An enjoyable entertainment was given,
by the International Brotherhood League
at Academy of Science Hall last evening.
Drs. Anderson and Griffiths told of what
the dub had accomplished in the way of
relief in Cuba. Several enjoyable
specialties were rendered by members of
the league, and at the close of the evening
a generous contribution was made by the
audience, to be used in purchasing sup
plies for the Cubans.
LOVELAND MADE
MANAGER OF THE
TRAFFIC LEAGUE
Coast Interests Are
Now United.
THREE STATES IN ACCORD
PREPARING FOR CONTEST AT
ST. LOUIS.
The First Gathering of Pacific Coast
Jobbers' and Manufacturers'
Association in This City
Was a Success.
The Pacific Coast Jobbers' and Manu
facturers' Association deliberated all yes
terday and reached final adjournment last
night. The work of organization was
completed. H. D. Loveland was elected
traffic manager for the Pacific Coast
and also chairman of the traffic commit
tee. The several sub-committees were
elected as follows:
Traffic Committeeâ Charles F. Tay of
the George H. Tay Co., A. C. Rulofson of
Baker & Hamilton, 1... Guggenheim of
Mack & Co., A. G. Towne of Blake, Mof
fltt & Towne, E. Everett of the Tubbs
Cordage Co., George Boole, manager of
Schwabacker Hardware Co. of Seattle;
Henry Hahn of Wadham & Co., Portland;
P. S. Harmon of the Harmon Furniture
I'm. of Tacoma; J. Baruch of Haas,
Baruch & Co., Los Angeles; William
Schaw of Schaw, Ingram & Baker, Sac
ramento.
Finance Committee â E. R. Lilicnthal of
the crown Distilleries Co., Charles Jiui
lirook of Holbrook. Merrill & Stetson, F.
W. Van Sicklen of Dodge, Sweeney &
Co., 1.. A. Lewis of Portland, J. S. Gold
smith of Schwabacker Hardware Co.,
Seattle; Charles A. Hyde of the West
Coast Grocery Co. of Tacoma, F. W.
Br,iun of P. W. Bnuin & Co. of Los An
geles: O. G. Bage of Sacramento.
An auditing committee was also elected,
consisting of the following: I. F. Little
fleld, A. L. Scoti and A. G. Towne of San
Francisco, C. 11. Black of the Seattle
Hardware Co., E. J. de Hart of Honey
man, de Hart & Co., Portland; Frederick
Mottet of Tacoma, J. O. Koepfli, Los An
geles: O. (i. Sag.:-. Sacramento.
K. S. Pillsbury, the association's attor
ney, was present at the afternoon session
and outlined the evidence which he wish
ed to have prepared for use at the hear
ing in St. Louis before the Interstate
Commerce Commission. The- members of
the traffic committee will get together the
necessary evidence in their respective
towns and forward it to Traffic Manager
Loveland.
The headquarters of the association will
be at 10 Pine street, accommodations hav
ing been offered by the Pacific Coast
Metal and Hardware Association. The
constitution and by-laws of the associa
tion have been somewhat modified since
the original adoption by the temporary
organization. Only work that is of gen
eral concern to the business interests of
the entire Pacific Cast will be taken up.
The lending sub-committee of the associa
tion in importance is the traffic commit
tee. To that was given entire charge of
the case at St. Louis, which is the first
important work before the association
President Wnkofield Baker and Traffic
Manager Loveland will go to -St Louis
and witnesses will be summoned from
the North and from the South. When the
members of the executive committee part
ed last night the work of welding- the
business interests of the Pacific Coast to
gether for the purpose of defense was
fully accomplished and a condition was
brought about that was unparalleled in
the history of the coast States.
Tf the Interstate Commerce Commission
should decide in favor of the St. Louis
Jobbers the case will be taken up to the
Supreme Court of the United States
which tribunal has laid down doctrine
concerning interstate commerce along the
lines advanced in the contention of the
Pacific Coast jobbers and manufacturers.
KOWALSKY MISTAKEN
FOR FRENNA, THE SLAYER
Colonel Henry I. Kowalsky came within
an ace of getting "pinched" late Sunday
evening, but in justice to the colonel it
might be said that he was thoroughly in
nocent of the position he occupied.
Sunday evening the colonel dropped his
portly form into a box in the Orpheum.
There was seated in the dress circle of the
theater a young man who from his ear
liest youth has known Joseph P, Frenna,
the murderer of James F. Turner. Ko
walsky has lost about twenty-five pounds
within the last few months and Frenna
has gained a similar amount of avoirdu
pois. Both now are about the same size,
both sport a black fringe on the upper
lip and both wear clothes. During the
long play the eyes of the young man
seldom wandered to the stage, and when
tho colonel tinally left the theater he was
shadowed by his discoverer. Kowalsky
didn't know it, and advanced along the
pavement. He drew a card from his
pocket and looked around the cabs for a
familiar number. It wasn't there, so he
continued along O'Farrell street, turned
into Powell and continued his course to
Geary. Again Colonel Kowalsky started
forward, reached Post street and hesitat
ed. Now was the time or never. The
young man who had followed him so
faithfully began a search for a policeman,
but before one was found the colonel had
taken a car, which sped away in the rain
just beginning to fall. The young man
who discovered him was chagrined at los
ing him so easily, but, undaunted, carried
the matter to Sheriff Martin yesterday
morning. Sheriff Martin was preparing to
clean out the County Jail, when one of
his sleuths found that Frenna was dozing
in his cell during theater hours on Sunday
night. Colonel Kowalsky, he also dis
covered, was the cause of all the trouble.

decided that expenses Incurred In a for

ANCIENT ORDER WORKMEN.
The Preparations for the Anniversary
"Celebration Next Friday.
The commfttep having: charge of the en
tertainment to be g^ven on the night of
Friday, the 27th inst.. in Odd Fellows'
Hall, in celebration of the anniversary of
the organization of the Ancient Order of
United Workmen, the pioneer of fraternal
beneficiary organizations of the United
States, has completed all its arrangements
for the event. The committee is: J. Col
lins grand master workman; Edwin Dan
forth deputy grand master workman;
Grand Master Snook. Walter Malloy, J.
H. McKnlpht and John T. Kidd.
An excellent programme of vocal, musi
cal and literary numbers has been ar
ranged. Past Grand Master Workman W.
1 1 Karnes will give a history of the or
der which in the past thirty odd years
has distributed nearly eighty-nine million
dollars to widows and orphans of deceased
members of the order and a large sum to
living members in sick benefits. He will
tell now more than thirty years ago
"Father" Upchuroh. in Meadville. Pa.,
wishing to help his fellow workmen, con
ceived the Idea of having them band to
gether, and by paying a small amount
monthly create a fund to be used in as
sisting widows and orphans.
From this small organization the ; ork
men have increased to more than 360,000,
and a number of other organizations
working on the same lines have sprung
into existence. Ex-Grand Recorder David
S Hirshberg will talk on the fraternal
feature of the Order of Workmen.

STILL MORE CLASSES.
-
IN his address in the Twenty-eighth Assembly Dis
tnct Mr. I ::eian proceeded to assume that labor
ing men would r.ot be allowed to vote. He there
upon worked himself into a hot box and excitedly
cried out: "I want you all to know that the Civu
Lode guarantees two nours xrom his jabor without i
loss of pay to every man. to vote at a general election, j
We snail insist upon this, but want no one to lose
his position by it, but the code says no one shall I
lose his position for it. I shail have it announced i;i j
the newspapers, after legal advice. I want you to
do this not for our sakes. but for yours, because we
are your friends, working for you."
By the way, Mr. Pheian of course has released
the iarge number of wage-workers employed in his j
industrial enterprises, to whom he pays wages. His ]
rent collectors, who promptly collect from his ten- I
ants, and his bank clerks, who figure interest on his '
gold-bearing mortgages, can march to the polls with |
conndence that their places will be open alter they ]
have used the red rubber stamp on the Australian
baliot. But he is afraid that the gentlemen who
merely employ thousands of common mechanics,
who by their wages support tens of thousands in i
their families, will not get a chance to vote .unless j
"I" consult a lawyer and publish it in the news-
The transparent demagogy of the Mayor, who em
ploys men mainly on his rent roll and interest com- I
putations, in suggesting to the honest wage-earners
â¢ . â â  . -. â¢ >
ot this city that only he stands between them and I
disfranchisement, is something to make the statues
en hi lawn slap their gypsum thighs and laugh like
the front row at a ballet.
Ihe Republican candidates want even' mechanic j
and workingmen to vote. Three years ago the la- J
boring men did not need Mr. Phelan's assistance and
the advice of his lawyer on that matter. They had
plenty ot leisure. There was no need of the code
to secure their positions after r.vo hours spent at the
polls, for they had no positions to lose, no waeeÂ«
to sacrifice. They were taking their turn for a day's j
work on the Balboa boulevard and other semi- I
charitable public enterprises, devised to keep them |
away from the public souphouse or the poorhouse. I
Mr. Phelan'3 party had been running things, and
while the great Pheian industry of rent collecting and-Â»
interest computation went on just the same, the great
wage-paving industries were dead.
i hey are now resurrected. Every day the call
goe9 out for men to earn the wages that are ready
for them to win. The allegiance of the working
man is not to men, but to conditions which he in
telligently comprehends. Mr. Phelan in his speech
accepting his nomination of himself said that if ha
is elected Mayor now, and his party wins, having
been faithful over few things it will be trusted over
many in the State and in the nation. This was a
plain declaration that a vote cast for him is a vote
affecting State and national politics. The working
men had a long, hard taste of his kind of national
politics. It gave them ample leisure to study the
effect. They do not wish any more of it just now,
and are interested in the conditions which suggest
another dose. They don't wish to build another
Balboa boulevard.

THE FIRST DAY'S FIGHT AT GLENCOE.
i \ J HEN" the account of the first day's fighting
\ \j at Glencoe, near Dundee, given in the dis
k * patch from Pretoria published in The Call
yesterday, is compared with that given out by the
British it will be that the conflict between the
antagonists on the field was not much greater than
that between the reports of the results. The compari
son is worth noting. Owing to the isolation of the
Transvaal nearly all the news from the war must
come from British sources or be transmitted by
! British lines of communication, and is therefore
liable to the coloring of British hopes or prejudices.
The evidence given in the Pretoria dispatch of ex
aggeration in the reports of the first battle will serve
to show to what extent allowance will have to be
made for the^e inevitable misrepresentations.
The British reports of the battle were to the effect
that a severe and almost a crushing defeat had been
inflicted upon the Boers. It was said: "There was
eight hours of continuous heavy firing. * * *
The British loss was severe, Lut that of the Boers
was much heavier. * * * The Boers got a re
verse which may possibly, for a time at any rate,
check all aggressive action." After describing the
charge up the hill and the capture of the position
occupied by the artillery of the Boers, the British
rts went on to say: '"As the Boers streamed
wildly down the hills, making for the main road,
they found their retreat had been cut off, but they
| rallied for a while and there was severe firing on
both sides. Many of them surrendered. A rough
estimate places the British loss at 250 killed and
wounded, and that of the Boers at 800."
The report from Pretoria giving an account of the
battle says: "The Transvaal Government received
this morning (October 21) the following dispatch
from Commander General Piet Joubert: 'Com
mander Lucas Meier has had an engagement at
Dundee. He made a plan of campaign with Com
mandant Erasmus by messenger. Erasmus, however,
â¢ 3 appear. It is estimated that the British lost
heavily. Our forces suffered, but owing to the mist
it hss been impossible to get all the details. It is re
â¢ ten of our forces were killed and twenty
rive v
Thus in a r- nfiict which the British report ss a
severe reverse to the Boers, the Boers report but
ten killed and twenty-five wounded, instead of the 800
estimated by the British. As to which of the two
accounts is the nearer accuracy something in the way
of light may be derived from the fact that instead of
7 being prevented from undertaking aggressive action
â  time the Boer? were able to renew the at
tack next day and are at this time pressing the Brit
ish hard and close not only at that point but at all
-ng the line of defense.
It is clear from this evidence that while the Brit
ish reports may be in the mam correct, they are not
to be relied upon so far as the estimates of the re
sults of the battles are concerned. Even- success of
their arms will seem to them a crushing defeat of the
Boers, and later news will be required to tell whether
the Boers themselves fee! it in that way.

THE CHARTER DECISION.
IN rebuking Examiner rant over the s.uit to test
the validity of the new charter we pointed out the
prime necessity of a judicial test of that instru
ment prior to its taking effect as the fundamental
of the city. Every man of common sense under
stood the need of arftrming it before it became opera
tive rat':- subjecting every municipal interest
to the suspense of waiting upon such a decision after
g ivernraent was organized according to its terras.
The decision is rendered. The charter is sustained
in all its parts and there is hereafter no temptation
to attempt to have it judicially picked to pieces by
tt law on any of its provisions or details.
It is now the fundamental law. It became so not
by the action or agency of any political party but
by the calm co-operation of all the separate depi
of authority, popular, leeislative, executive and ju
dicial, by law charged with the power to join in its
production. It is easy of enforcement and will be
enforced to the line and the letter, for the public
opinion which ran throughout its creation will stand
back of its administration.

candy man, too, sweet thing!
TRUSTS AND CURRENCY REFORM
SENATOR ALLISON in opening the Repub
lican campaign in lowa was notably strong in
his declarations concerning the action like to
be taken by the Republicans of Congress this winter
in dealing with questions of currency reform and the
regulation of trusts, those two issues having been
given considerable importance in the campaign by
the Democrats, who have charged the Republican
party with being favorable to trusts and with playing
fast and loose with the financial issue. These mat
ters are of concern to the whole nation and it is
therefore gratifying that the Senator spoke of them
rather than of some local question, for no 'Senator
can speak for Republicanism with more authority
than Allison.
"The Republican party," said he, "has not hesi
tated to deal with the question of trusts in the past
ill not shrink from dealing with it now." He
went on to point out that the issue has been raised
by the natural evolution of our economic life and
that the solution of its problems requires the highest
abilities and the best purposes of earnest student;.
At present it is not and cannot be made a party ques
tion, since all parties are agreed that trusts should
be subject to regulation for the purpose of prevent
ing such evils as may develop in them, but no party
a> yet has formulated any definite plan or policy of
regulation. Congress, said he, and the various State
legislatures will after full debate legislate in such a
way as to minimize if not wholly eradicate the per
nicious features of such combinations, and to that
end the best men of both parties should and doubt
â  ;s will work together.
The currency question is a party question and
Senator Allison confirmed the expectations of those
who look to the Republican majority in Congress
â¢.=;ure currency reform at the coming session.
Upon that point he said:
"It is the purpose of the Republican party, which
has constantly sustained our system of sound money
based upon the gold standard, to continue to main
tain it â all of it. whether silver or paper â at a parity
with and convertible directly or indirectly into gold
at the will of the holder, and it will be maintained in
the future, not by the voice of party, but by the voice
of the American people, and the laws upon our
statute books upon this subject will be invigorated
rind strengthened to maintain it and preserve it, and to
make it impossible for any Secretary of the Treasury
or any President by his own fiat and without positive
affirmative legislation of Congress to force upon the
people in some hour of temporary depression or of
national slumber the depreciated standard of silver
money, or place any of our money at a premium or
discount."
It is to be noted that Congressman Henderson,
who will be the Speaker of the House, in a recent
speech has taken exactly the ground of Senator Alli
son on both these iÂ«sues. "There should be abso
lutely no party politics," he said, "on the trust ques
tion. The party or person who attempts to make
political capital out cf it is neither wise nor fair. The
party that does it is controlled by demagogues and
the person who does it is a demagogae." Concern
ing the financial .question, he said: "I predict that a
Republican Congress will legislate at the coming
session so as to increase the confidence of the peo
ple, establish firmly the gold standard of the civil
ized world and let the people know that all their
obligations will be discharged on a safe, firm basis."

"HOflE STUDY CIRCLE."
COGSWELL POLYTECHNIC COLLEGE,

SAN FRANCISCO, Oct. 7, 1899. r â r~ '
Editor of The Call: In a time
when every earthly and unearthly J
temptation to idleness, and worse, j 7* Jft^m
is fluttering its most alluring J
colors and fashions in the face
of the young, half - formed mechan- *%^ ||
ie and clerk, a counter to them ! B||
like that proposed by The Call j
must be welcomed by every man who 1
has eyes or ears and uses them.
Nor should we forget that the w H v ' RAYMoND -
student body of the Home Study Circle is a
body that will never cease to challenge the
admiration of the world while it numbers
among its members such names as Michael An-
gelo, Elihu Burritt, Benjamin Franklin, Hugh

it, even as the sons of the university are
loyal to their alma mater. Yours truly,

HOW LIPTON
GOT HIS START
IN THE WORLD
MUCH Is being published about Sir
Thomas Lipton ai this timeâ about
his origin, his birth, his rise, his
tastes, affiliations, passions and
prejudices. I have met him sev
eral times, and think I know him fairly j
well. I have not met him since he became j
Sir Thomas; he was plain Mr. Lipton '
APPRECIATED THE CALL'S MAP OF THE WAR
whe-n I last encountered him. and traveled
; in the same Pullman coach with him from
El Pa.=o, Texas, to the city of Washington.
I should think this was about five years
; ago.
It has been asserted time and again, ana
I frequently on the alleged authority of Sir
Thomas, that he was born in Glasgow,
'â â  Scotland, though of Irish parentage. Sir
Thomas told me that he was born in the
town of Dungannon, county of Tyrone.
; Ireland, and that his people lived in Ire
[ land. He was taken to Scotland by his
' parents in his infancy and they settled in
I Glasgow. He was given five years at the
! public schools, and then was apprenticed
!in a big mercantile establishment. His
1 duties here for the first few years were
j various. He was kicked and cuffed, of
course. He attended to the windows, he
â¢ up the floors, and polished up the
I handles of the big front doors.
At the end of his first year's service he '
j found himself with half a crown anead. !
; A half-crown is a British silver coin, |
sometimes called "two-and-six," and ap- j
proxirr.ates closely to our 50-cent piece, j
He was happy. The coin did not get an j
: opportunity to burn his pocketâ he salted }
'< It away. The second year he had a little
| more money; the third year more still. ;
and finally at the end of five years and his â 
' apprenticeship he was the absolute owner i
j of Â£10, rousjhiy speaking $50. But he had
accumulated a whole lot of worldly ex- ;
perlence, wisdom, and the craft of com
merce.
Let it be understood that I am reciting
this history as he told it to me himself in 1
! th* long journey between El Paso and i
I Washington. When he found himself a !
, free man with Â£10 "to boot" an irresistible <
'â  desire to visit his birthplace at Dungan- ,
! non, Ireland, possessed him. He ascer- i
.' talned that it would take nearly one-fifth !
â of his capital to make the journey. He j
â  knew right well that the cost of his sus- !
tenance while at Dungannon need not be ;
I taken into account. He left for his native i
I place, being then 17 years old. He told
! me at length the delights of his visit. \
\ How simple and industrious and prudent
: and patriotic hi? relatives and their neigh-
I bors were; about his viÃits to the old
; church in Dungannon, where the historic
! convention of the Irish volunteers of 1752
: was held that demanded and wonâ for a
timeâ the legislative Independence of Ire
i land.
But wh.it attracted the commercial side
I of him was the Irish bacon. It goes with
j out saying that a. cookc-d pig is best when
:he is Irish. This If not only a maxim in
i trade but of the palate. Making inquiries
. young Llpton discovered that the rafters
I of every Irish farmhouse for miles around
I were laden with bacon, that there was no
(market for'it, and consequently no com
'< merclal value. Lipton. who had concluded
j to splurge a few shillings of his "pile" to
i make an impression on his old neighbors,
â  changed his mind and invested every pen
! Ny he had in Irish bacon. He carried It
1 back to Glasgow with him in a good
i sized box as luggage. He knew well the
; tastes and character of the principal pa
trons of the establishment where he
; served his apprenticeship, and to these he
displayed and sold his Irish bacon. He
1 cleared Â£10 in this, his first speculation.
He lo?t no time in returning to Ireland
and securing an option on all the bacoa in
the vicinity of Dungannon. Thus he laid
the foundation of his fortune.
Sir Thomas was given the handle to his
name Fcon after the Queen's Diamond
! Jubilee. There are two kinds of sirs in
I Great Britain. One kind is of the baronet
1 class, hereditary and regarded as semi
: aristocratic. The other, called knights,
: nearly anybody can attain who does any-
I thing successful. The usual route to this
I latter honor is to make a contribution to
some charity in which royalty is inter
ested, or to the campaign chest of the
party in power.
On the occasion of the Queen's Jubilee
the Princess of Wales parented the idea
i of giving a big feed including pie and un
i usual incidentals to the poor of Lon
; <3on. She appealed for funds and the re
j sponse was not gratifying nor s^pontane
i ous. The scheme was on the verge of col
lapse when Lipton went forward with a
I contribution of $125,000. Soon afterward
I he was dubbed Sir Thomas.
He has large interests In this country.
i He owns immense cattle raising ranches
j n Texas and Montana and is largely mi
i terested in more than one of the big pack
i ing establishments of Chicago.
| Sir Thomas does not readily trtl his age,
' but I shu uld Judge that he was in the
â  neighborhood of 53 years. He is well pre
â¢ served. His mustache is of good growth,
' and he shaves himself every morning. So
I expert he !s at shaving that he did it on
1 a Baltimore and Ohio train east of Graf
i ton, where it requires a gymnast almost
Ito hold his seat. He is an all-round good
1 fellow, and would remind one of a Central
: Western American rather than of a
â  Briton. There is no txclusiveness about
: him. He is hearty in his manners, likes
i an occasional glass of whisky nr a bottle
j of ale and smokes both cigars and a pipe.
' In British politics he is a thorough
Irish Nationalist. He will probably be a
member of Parliament after the next gen
eral election. About a year ago Veaey
Knox. a Nationalist, resigned the repre
sentation of Derry in Parliament and the
Irish committee offered the seat to Sir
Thomas. He hesitated for a few days
and concluded not to enter Parliament
then, but promised to go in as a thorough
Nationalist at the next general election.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 25, 1899.
SCANDAL'S BLIGHT ON
THE HOTEL RAFAEL
Baron yon Schroeder Destroyed
It as a Reputable Resort
General Warfield Demands and Secures a
Release of His Obligation as Man
ager and Lessee.
General R. H. Warfield will, on Novem- I
ber 1, surrender his lease of the Hotel \
Rafael to Baron yon Schroeder, the own- !
er of the property. To the general public !
this retirement of General Warneld will
be a surprise, but to those who have had '
an opportunity of observing events at the j
San Rafael hostelry, the expected has |
happened. General Warneld found it im
possible to conduct a respectable hotel
and to permit Baron yon Schroeder to |
enter the doors. The general has decided,
therefore, to retire from the contract that
was neither pleasant nor profitable.
He could not ask patronage of reputa- j
ble men and women and at the same I
time wink at the escapades of the Baro-n. I
It was impossible to ask decent men and
women who honor their homes to reside
under the same roof where gambling and
outrageous revelry made noisy the hours
of the night and shocked the senses of
respectable people. The Baron insisted ]
upon having his way and the other guests I
of General Warfleld indulged their privi- I
lege of moving out. Scandal threw its I
shadow over the establishment, and in j
that shadow General Warfleld counted j
his losses. The Baron became altogether !
too expensive a luxury and General War
field determined if possible to dispense
with him.
He therefore asked that he be permitted
to retire from the obligation of his lease
before its legal termination.
Baron yon Schroeder granted the !
request, and it is presumed he will i
conduct the hotel on the principles that j
made it odious to the people who once
favored it with their patronage. The
Baron has full liberty now to conduct his
gambling parties as frequently as he
pieasea and with whom he pleases. Il
ls free to manage it in what- I
ever way will best suit his manners and |
morals, and perhaps may nhviate the ne
cessity of having his brother booted from j
any more local hotels. The trouble be
tween Baron yon Schroeder and General
Warfield is of long standing.
When General Wariiehl leased the Ho-
I tel Rafael it was with the understanding j
; that the establishment was to be kept
open the entire year. After one year's
experience the manager discovered his
sum of profits were eaten by his winter
I losses and he asked permission to close
! the hotel .hiring the winter and make it
1 distinctively a summer resort. To this
Baron yon Schroeder would not agre.Â».
He Insisted the place be kept open the
entire year. Warfield demurred, but un
der the conditions of his lease was
forced to submit, although he went so far
as to offer Yon Schroeder the rent of the
place for the entire year with the prlvl-

legje of keophiEf it open only during tho
summer months.
Even to this more than fair proposal
Baron yon Sclirocder would not agree.
This was the first but by no means the
most serious cause of the trouble which
led Â«P to the present retirement of War
field. When the hotel first opened every
possible effort was made to make It a
fashionable summer resort for reputable
people.
The Baron and his wealthy wife went
there and helped things along with a
monthly bill of $1100. They remained for
two years, and shortly afterward Baron
ess yon Schroeder went to Germany.
It was the general expectation that her
husband would quickly follow her, but
he found here company more congenial
and remained. Affairs In the Yon
Schroeder household had not been mov
ing as smoothly as might have been de-
Bired. The Baron was expensive. He i
spent money lavishly, and an estate \
which was estimated at over three mil
lion dollars began to dwindle until it
looked like four hundred and fifty thou
sand. A million dollars in bonds of the
Donahue railroad had gone. Gossip
tongues were wagging, and justly bo, for
the Baron took no pains to observe those
conventionalities which hide sometimes
even derelictions.
The Baroness, berore she left for Eu
rope, gave her power of attorney to At
torney Giselman, without whose signature
Baron Yon Schroeder cannot dispose of

hear a lecture by Rev. Joseph Sasia, S. J.,
entitled "A Wonderful Subject," under
the auspices of Columbia Council No 55
rounij Men's Institute. The programme
â¢ opened with a piano solo by A. E. Magill
â¢ Jr. D. J. Sheehan. president of the coun
cil, made a few Introductory remarks, and
John Desmond sang two tenor solos In
excellent voice.
In his lecture Father Sasia stated that
the subject as announced was but another
name for miracles, which no doubt his
auditors had already realized. He
prefaced his remarks by alluding to the
wonderful physical laws of nature which
regulated the phenomena of the vegetable
animal and human kingdoms, but which
in themselves were submissive Ito tne
hand who had created these laws
"It is the temporary suspension of these
laws, said the reverend lecturer, "by the
Creator whereby he performs miracles in
order to exhibit his divine omnipotence
and to manifest his glory to the world
God performs and has performed actions
above and beyond the ordinary course of
things, as has been attested by the in
! disputable evidence of reliable witnesses
But it is a fact that only the power whir i
has created these physical laws possesses
the wonirous power of suspending them
at will.
Father Sasia then proceeded to prove
three points which sum up the whole
proposition as follows: First, miracles
are possible and can be performed; sec
ond, miracles have absolutely and un
deniably occurred; third, miracles cannot
be accounted for by any material agent
or attributed to any created being but re
j quired for that purpose the omnipotent
i hand of God.
