The four-day session of the forty-third
annual meeting of the General Associa
tion of California was commenced in the
First Congregational Church yesterday
afternoon. The convocation opened with
devotions conducted by Rev. G. A. Jas
per of Humboldt and after roll call or
ganization was effected by the election
of Rev. W. W. Bcudder of Alameda as
moderator; Rev. R. H. Sink of Stockton,
assistant moderator; Rev. G. A. Jasper,
scribe, and Rev. H. W. Benham of Clover
dale, assistant scribe. There were 200
delegates present.
The reports of delegates to other bodies
were read as follows: To Congregational
Association of Southern California, Rev.
J. K. McLean and Rev. W. W. Scudder;
to Hawaiian Association. Rev. G. B.
Hatch; to Synod of the Pacific, Rev. W.
A. Tenney; to Methodist Episcopal Con
ference, Rev. W. H. Cross; to Methodist
Episcopal South Conference, Rev. L. D.
Rathbone; to Central Baptist Association,
Hon. J. M. Haven; to Cumberland Pres
byterian Synod, Rev. Francis l^awson.
Rev. J. T. Ford brought greetings from
the General Association of Southern Cali
fornia. He stated that the Congrega
tional College of Pomona was out of debt
and had recently received an endowment
of IIOO.ijOO, but declared that this was not
enough, and he made an urgent appeal to
Students from Congregationalists in the
northern jurisdiction for assistance. Rev.
A. D. Pelt bpoke for the southern part
of the State, and Professor C. F. Nash
addressed the convocation as a delegate
from the State Association.
Rev. P.. F. Sargent read the report of
the visitors to the Theological Seminary,
In which he made a strong plea for a
closer relationship between the seminary
and the churches. H.e advised that one
half of the trustees t>e appoined by the
churches. A discussion followed, partici
pated in by Rev. Philip Coombe and Dr.
George Mooar, in which the latter said
that the proposition of moving the semi
nary to Berkeley was being considered,
but' there was considerable opposition to
it, and the matter was in abeyance.
On the recommendation of the nomi
nating committee the following commit
tees were appointed:
Business committeeâ Rev. G. B. Hatch,
W. M. Searby and Rev. I* J. Garver.
Committee on rollâ Rev. Charles Kirk
land, Rev. H. E. Jewett and Mrs. A. Mot
ley.
Press committeeâ Rev. William Rader
and Rev. H. E. Barham.
The afternoon session concluded with
an address on "The Organization of the
State Association," by Rev, James H.
Warren, D. D., who traced the growth of
the association from the year 1552. when
there were but three churches in Cali
fornia, In 1557. he saM, when the Gen
eral Association was organised on an in
dependent basis, there were twelve
churches rind nine delegates. Now there
were 220 churches and about 250 minis
ters. , _ â 
Rev. Dr. Pond, at the request of Dr.
Warren, then spoke of the early fellow
ship which prevailed among the Congre
gational-Presbyterian brethren.
In the evening Rev. J. R. Knodell of
Oakland preached the associational ser
mon. Rev. George C. Adams, pastor of
the First Congregational Church, deliv
ered an address of welcome, to which
Rev. H. Melville Teney responded in a
few well-chosen words.
The session will be resumed this morn
ing at 8:30 o'clock.

THE CALLS
HOME STUDY CIRCLE.
DESK STUDIES FOR GIRLS.
Copyright, 1899, by Seymour Eaton.
1. NEW WORDS AND HOW TO
USE THEM.
(Continued.)
As a rule it is unwise to use a foreign
word if an English word will express the
intended meaning. There are, however, a ;
j few foreign words which have come into
common use and for which there are no
English equivalents. Our tendency in
language as well as in business is toward
"short cuts." When a foreign word will
express what in English would require an
elaborate sentence the foreign word is to
be preferredâ that is, of course, if the lis
tener understands its meaning. Note the
following:
Esprit de corps. Pronounced "es-pre-de
core. ' Esprit means spirit. This French
phrase is cummonly used by English-speak
ing people in the sense of common uevot
edness, sympathy or support among the
members of an association or a military
company or of any body of men united
for any purpose. Thus we may say, "The
esprit de corps of the London police is
worthy of imitation."
Coup d'etat. Pronounced "coo-day-tah,"
with the last syllable accented. A Dold or
brilliant stroke of statesmanship; an un- ,
expected stroke of policy. A daring move
by which one political party outwits or
defeats another.
Finesse. Pronounced "fi-ness," with the
second syllable accented. This word in ,
French means "fineness," and is com
monly used by English-speaking people to
denote artfulness, craft or adroitness; that
quality of mind which leads to subtle
actions. Note this illustration: "Her use
of finesse in the most trivial affairs gave
one the impression of being constantly
i duped or imposed upon."
Vade mecum. Pronounced "va-de-me
kum." A. book or article which a person
carries with him as a constant companion.
It may be a Baedeker or perhaps a flask
of brandy.
Siesta. Pronounced "see-es-ta." A nap
or period of rest in the middle of the day.
! The hour of siesta is strictly observed in
j the warm countries of Southern Europe.
Bete noir. Pronounced "bate-nwor."
The literal meaning is "black beast." The
expression is commonly used to denote a
pi-t dislike, something which makes us
especially uncomfortable.
These are sufficient to illustrate the
point. Let us now look at a few English
words which we may find to our advan
tage to add to our vocabulary. There is
jno copyright on language. Words are
absolutely free. Custom simply requires
that we use them correctly:
Cynic. Pronounced as spelled. This
! word originally meant dogllke. A cynic
!is a sneering faultfinder. He shows a
disposition to disbelieve or doubt the sin
cerity of others. We say a man is cynical
or that he makes a cynical remark, or '
: has a cynical laugh, when we refer to
; some relation or expression or intimation
which questions the motives or thoughts
of others.
Optimist. Pronounced as spelled. An
' optimist Is one who believes in the ulti
! mate supremacy of good over evil. He
! always hopes for and expects the best. :
;He has a large and broad outlook. His
hopeful disposition keeps him In the best
of cheer.
Pessimist. Pronounced "pess-i-mist,'*
; with the accent upon the first syllable.
; This word means the opposite of "opti
mist." A pessimist is one who exagger
-1 ates the evils of life, or who Is disposed :
to look only on the dark side of things. ,
1 He takes a melancholy or depressing view
;of life. A pessimist In religion believes
i that this world is bad and its tendency is
1 to grow worse rather than better.
! Cant. Affectation in language. If it is i
i evident to the educated or cultured or ex- .
! perienced man that a particular writer or i
speaker does not really mean what he j
says, does not act It out in his own life;
that he is simply making a respectable
I show of words or of thought for appear- !
' ance sake; if this is clear, the speech or \
article is properly called political or re
ligious cant. The word signifies a whin
! ing pretension of goodness; an insincere -
SHOP AND TRADE STUDIES FOR BOYS.
Copyright, 1599, by Seymour Eaton.
I. ARITHMETIC OF THE WORK
SHOP.
Lesson No. 2.
In our first lesson we considered boards
one inch thick and called a square foot a
board foot. A board a foot square and an
inch thick contains 144 cubic inches, so
that if we know the number of cubic
inches in a wood solid we can find the
number of board feet by dividing by 144.
Note the following exercises:
1. How many cubic inches in a block of
wood 2 feet square and 7 inches thick?
2. How many cubic inches in a stick of
square timber 8 inches by 10 Inches and 20
feet long?
3. How many board feet in five sticks of
timber oach 16 inches by 18 Inches and 12
feet long?
4. The lumber of the floor of a piazza of
the shape shown in the diagram is 14
inrhos thick. AB Is 40 feet; CD is 25 feet;
AE Is 36 feet; CG Is 21 feet. Find the
value of the lumber at $48 a thousand
feet.
5. There are 10 supports required for a
bridge. They are all to be of the same
size and of the shape shown In the figure.
A is 18 feet long and 16 inches square; B
and C are each 15 feet long and 16 inches
square. They are billed at the rate of
$48 p p r 1000 board feet. Find the total
amount of the bill.
Noteâ To find the area of a circle mul
tiply half of the diameter (AB) by itself
and multiply the product by 3 1-7. To find
the circumference of a circle multiply the
diameter by 3 1-7.
6, Find the value of the lumber in a cir
cular platform 210 feet in diameter and
use of religious phraseology: an affecta
tion of piety; a sham interest or enthusi-
Ism; false pretenses in society customs.
Finale. Pronounced "fee-naugh-lee,"
with the accent upon the middle syllable.
The concluding section of a piece of mu
sic or of any act of a dramatic work like
an opera, especially if so managed as to
produce an effective climax. The last
piece on a programme: the last part,
piece or scene in any public performance
or exhibition. The concluding act might
properly be called a grand finale.
Travesty. A travesty Is a raricature, a
burlesque, a grotesque or deceitful imita
tion. Our minstrel shows, for instance,
find a drawing card in making a travesty
of some current event or popular drama.
In literature a travesty is a burlesque
treatment of a noble Subjectâ the opposite
of a parodyâ or serious treatment of a
trivial subject. We say that a particular
thing said or dene by another is a trav
esty upon a man's character If the man
is represented Improperly or in burlesque.
Glamour. Pronounced "glam-ur." This
word means illusion; an artificial interest
or association causing things to appear
different from what they really are. Thus
we refer to some of the beautiful stories
which we find recorded in the life of
Washington as the glamour of history.
Sinecure. Pronounced "sy-ne-kure." The
literal meaning is "without care." and
therefore the name is applied to a position
having attached large pay and requiring
little work. Political offices are too often
looked upon by their holders as sinecures.
Correct speech is learned only by care
ful study. We must watch ourselves con
stantly until good English becomes a
habit. When reading it is wise to read
aloud as much as possible. There 1b no
better way of becoming accustomed to
the sound and rhythm of words. Their
proper grammatical relation soon becomes
as familiar to the ear as correct spelling
does to the eye. The ear immediately de
tects a false construction or improperly
used word just as the eye notes a wrong
combination of letters in the spelling.
Note the following cautions:
The words each, every, either and neith
er are followed by singular verbs, as:
"Each of the boys was there." "Either
he or his brother was to be appointed."
"1 am not certain that either of us was
there." "Let every one please himself,"
not "themselves." When singular names
connected by "and" are preceded by each,
every or no the verb must be singular, as:
"Each book and slate was in position."
'"Every man and woman was drowned."
"No chair or desk is to be used in the
room."
As well as and none take singuar verbs.
It is wrong to say "Greek as well as Latin
were studied." It should be "was
studied." "None of up are going" should
be "None of us is going."
Be careful to use the possessive form hi 3
instead of him in such sentences as "I
did not think of his returning so soon."
"If I were in his place." In sentences of
this character use were and not was. It
is incorrect to say "If I was he." Say "If
I were he." "If he was in Tom's shoes
he would think differently." Say "If he
were." It may be put down as a general
rule that in .sentences denoting supposi
tion or doubt the plural form of the verb
should be used.
The words lie and lay are often incor
rectly used. To He means to place one's
self in a horizon taj position; to lay means
to put or place, to spread on the surface.
The book lies on the table, not lays on
the table. I lay down for a few minutes,
not I laid down. I have lain down, not I
have laid down. I have laid it down, not
I have lain it down.
The word either means, strictly, the one
or the other of two; both means two
taken collectively, and each means two
considered separately.
Where this and that, these and those,
the one and the other refer to things
previously mentioned, this and these refer
to the last mentioned, and that and those
to the first mentioped. the one refers to
the first mentioned and the other to the
last mentioned.
The words answer and reply should not
be used indiscriminately. An answer Is
given to a question, a reply to an asser
tion. When we are addressed we answer;
when we are accused we reply.

EASTERN MARKETS,
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Oct. 24.â The etock market was
distinctly reactionary to-day In spite of some
etrong spots. Profit taking was on a very large
rcale. Sales ' d this account were quite well
Bbporbed and created no sign of demoralisa
tion in prices. When the fact of profit taking
became obvlouÃ some, of the room traders
turned bears and fold the market moderately.
The bear faction was not aggressive, however,
and made no great inroads upon values. Neither
were the bulls inclined to resume their cam
paifrn for the advance after having taken their
1 : fits and the markets were inclined to drag
during the latter part of the day. Some at
tempt was made to Infuse strength into the
mnriiet in the last hour by bidding up Union
Pacific and Baltimore A Ohio preferred. Busi
ness induced some slight covering by shorts,
but so far as a general resumption of the ad
vance wns concerned the attempt was abortive.
Prices were very unsteady at the close and
In many cases were pursuing a downward
course. The active realizing in the first hour
was much helped by a notabis accession of
outside demand, which was attract"! by yes
terday'B sharp rise following a week of stub.
born resistance to all attempts at driving prices
downward. This demand and continued ma
nipulation by a section of the bull party served
to wipe out pretty generally the opening de
cline find bring- the market back to about last
night's level. Having reached that point the
downward course began and with the exception
of coma irregular fluctuations the market con
tinued heavy, below last night's level for the
rest of the day. Special points of weakness in
the early dealings were Sugar, American To
bacco Malting preferred. Anaconda, Pullman
and Bro6k!yn Transit. Aggressive strength
â was shown all day by united States Leather,
which was taken in enormous lots and roee at
one time to 22*Â«. The closing waa a point be
low that. New York Central, after a small
fractional advance, met very heavy offerings
on the announcement of an additional stock
If3u* of $15,000,000. The stock moved somewhat
Irregularly during the day, within a range of
o point below last night's level. The London
market reflected some depression upon this
market and the undertone there was distinctly
weak on the uneasiness regarding the position
of thÂ« British troops in Natal.- The suggestion
or the Chancellor of the Exchequer that a tax
! on South African gold mines might be looked >
j to to help defray the expenses of the war was
' apparently not relished by the speculators in
the mine market. London sales In this market
; were upward of 30,000 shares. The general |
! weakness of copper mine shares had a de- 1
pressing influence on speculation. The weak- j
â  ness of Anaconda on the exchange was supple- j
mented by a break in Amalgamated Copper In ]
the outside market and by weakness in Bos- ,
ton. The hardening tendency of money was a j
; discouraging factor for the bulls. Most of the 1
day's call loans were made above 6 per cAit I
, and the rate rose to 8 per cent In the day. !
Bankers generally asked 6 per cent for time ;
: loans and were disinclined to put out their
; funds for the longer periods. Rather less
; money was shipped to the interior to-day, but
the outgo, while diminished, still continues.
The sub-treasury Is also absorbing funds.
Realizing was In evidence In some portions
of the bond market, making the price changes
mixed. Total sales, par value, $2,400,000.
United States old 4's advanced *4 and the
new 4's and s's hi In the bid price.

Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO, Oct. 24.â Disappointing cables \
started wheat rather easy. The market soon I
I began to harden, however, as shorts com
j menced covering from the outset, owing: to re
ports of continued drought in the Southwest,
where much of the fall-sown wheat has not i
yet germinated. Rumors of a sharp break in i
consols caused further apprehension and as i
outside orders began to flood the pit the de- â¢
mand became so urgent that a %c advance was ;
! added to the opening figures during the first
twenty minutes' trading. After that the ups !
I and downs were frequent within a rather nar- ,
row ranee until the last hour, when an in- I
i crease In the world's visible of 4.875,000 bushels, I
where only about 2,000.000 bushels had been ex-
I pected, started liquidation and the market
soon lost -Its previous buoyancy. Reports of a
break in the drought added to the selling move
ment and the early gain was lost. : The mar
ket continued weak the remainder of the ses
j sion and closed at the bottom. A report that
Argentine would have a surplus of over 80.
--000,000 bushels for export this year had a de
pressing influence during the late trading.

WHEATâ The feeling was unsettled In sym

| The demand was fair.

was %d higher and consols %d lower. Buyers

were strong at an advance of He over Monday.

plus of the coming crop to be 70,000.000 bushels,
with 16.000,000 of the old crop still on hand.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. Tha
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tldÂ»
and the * st or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are in addition to the soundings on the
Lnited States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (-) precedes the height,
and then thÂ« number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

ALLIES OF WORKINGMEN
KTW CHARTER DECISION
CLEARS WAY TO VICTORY.
large and Confident Audiences
Greet Horace Davis and Other
Candidates Every
Night.
The outlook for complete Republican
success at the polls on Tuesday, Novem
ber 7, daily grows brighter. The decision
of the Supreme Court sustaining the new
charter is an inspiration to Republican
workers. Certain Phelanites who have
been trying to arouse apprehension that
the charter was in danger and in need of
the protecting care of the Committee of
One Hundred find that their chief source
of political capital has vanished,
The charter is now secure. It has been
adopted by the people, ratified by the
legislature and its validity is established
by the Supreme Court of the State. It is
now the charter of San Francisco and not
the special property of any gang of place
hunters masquerading as reformers to
catch non-partisan votes.
The Republican party has demonstrated
Its ability to manage the affairs of the
nation and the State, and Republicans
believe that the party is capable of direct
ing the municipal affairs of San Fran
cisco. From the beginning of the charter
agitation until the rinish the charter had
no abler exponents and supporters than
Horace Davis and I. J. Truman. To these
gentlemen and others on the Republican
ticket the citizens owe a debt of gratitude
for intelligent work performed in behalf
of the local constitution. : V- " .
Mayor Phelan is now hedging on the i
proposition that Republicans opposed the
charter lie asserts that he dirt not make
the charge, but with a wave of the hand
and a smile of insinuation cites Joseph
Uritton as authority for the statement. â 
Phelan did charge Republicans with dis
loyalty to the charter, and now brings
Joseph R. Brltton forward as State's evi
dence. Were not the Republicans sincere
in their devotion to the charter when they
placed on their ticket for Supervisor the ;
man salted by Phrlan as the ideal cham
pion of the charter? Did not the nomina- !
tion of Britton attest the sincerity of Re- !
publicans? On what ground, then, can j
Phelan contend- that Republicans were
disloyal?
At headquarters of the Republican
County Committee and the room of the !
Horace Davis Central Club, indications of I
Republican unity and success are ob
served. Out in the districts the party
meetings are large and enthusiastic. In i
the beginning of the campaign fear was
expressed that sufficient interest could
not be awakened in the municipal elec
tion to induce registered Republicans to
turn out ami participate in the demonstra- |
tions of the campaign. The decision of ;
the court holding that all the principal |
offices are to be tilled gives the local con
test the prestige of a general election
and insures a very large vote. In San â 
Francisco a full vote may be accepted as !
a guarantee of Republican triumph. The
fact that the approaching municipal elec- |
tion will be regarded throughout the State
of California and In the East as an en
gagement preliminary to the great battle
of 1900, will stimulate Republicans to
special effort en behalf of the ticket.
I'ndor the leadership of Horace Davis a
groat victory should be gained. He is a I
leader of national reputation, fearless,
able and honest. :

POSTUM CEBEAL.
"BAD AS LIQUOR HABIT," '
Druggist Says of Ooflee Drinking.
"Drinking coffee at restaurants more
or loss for several yenrs, I was finally
forced to the conclusion that it was
the cause of my dyspepsia, with the
hungry feeling I was continually an-
I with. It caused me not only
distress, but s<-rious Inconvenience. I
am satisfied that the coffee drinking
habit U v truly a pernicious habit as
the drinking of liquor. It made me ex-
ceedingly nervous and interfered with
my business, where a Bteady hand is
a necessity.
"My experience with Postum Food
Coffee teaches that it is truly a blesÃ-
lng, and to be highly recommended.
My own great improvement has given
me interest in the preparation. Mr.
Frank Terrey, a cyclist, at 6713 Vin-
cennes avenue, left off coffee and drank
only Postum during his training 1 forth*
races and was greatly benefited by the
change. C. R. Paterson, another cy-
ueed Postum in place of coffee
nnd saye it gives strength and vigoÂ»
whore coffee causes a feeling of heavi-
ness."â Edgar G. Coates, 86 Van Buren
Btruet, Chicago, IIL
structed his good people patronizingly
how to vote. Charles Wesley Reed mod
estly refrained from a long speech, as he
said he would simply say what the Mayor
would say later and he did not want to
steal any of his Honor's thunder.
Major Geary was even more fiery' than
usual. He has got out on the firing line
now and says he is going to win the bat
tle aa he won battles at Manila. As he
won none there his self-imposed task
ought to be comparatively easy from one
point of view. Dr. R. Beverly Cole re
peated the chaperone performance and
related Incidents of the childhood days
of. the other candidates. As the hour
grew late the difficulty of holding the au
dience became greater. Max Popper, the
chairman, filled in an awkward gap with
a dissertation on the charter and at last
the Mayor came.
Mayor Phelan had no new Ideas to of
fer. He Is still the apostle of progress,
the champion of the people's rights, the
defender of the municipality. Every
thing that is bad or weak In local gov
ernment is the work of his opponents.
Everything good that was ever won for
San Francisco was won by himself ami
he pledged his audience that he will
win some more if they let him. He wants
to be Mayor not for gain nor for honor,
but to serve the people. When the
Mayor had concluded the meeting ad
journed with three cheers for the ticket.
ENTHUSIASTIC RALLY.
Citizens of ths Potrero Gather
and Shout for Horace
Davis.
A rousing meeting: was held last night
by the Thirty-second Republican District
i Slab. Bonfires and music acted as har
bingers and the hall was crowded before
the hour for the candidates' speeches had
arrived. George Wimmer acted as chair
man of the evening.
When Horace Davis, the Republican
candidate for Mayor, walked into the hall
he was greeted with prolonged cheers, but
this was hut a mite compared with the
applause and welcome he received -when
introduced by Chairman Wimmer. Every
period of the Republican candidate was
the signal for cheering and hand-clapping
approbation en the part of the audience,
which was most enthusiastic.
Horace Davis toid his audience of his
position in favor of the charter, of his
career and his solicitude for the â¢working
men He also referred to his labors in
Congress and his success in securing the
passage of the exclusion bill. Taxes and
sewerage Improvement were also consid
ered, and Mr. Davis' views on the one
which advocates a reduction and not an
Increase and a much improved sanitary
st-werage for the city showed accord with
the minds 01 his audience.
Following Horace Davis, Major C. W .
Kyle took the platform, and he spoke of
what the Republican candidate had done
for the working classes and what his ca
reer as a business man had been. "Every
dollar he earned." said Major Kyle, "has
been put into productive industries which
give employment to his fellow-men."
L. Grant Carpentar, nominee for Police
Judge, took the platform. and his remarks
called for lengthy applause. Dr. T. H.
Morris, nominee for Supervisor; Dr. Mc-
Lean, nominee for Supervisor, and other
candidates spoke on campaign issues, and
the meeting adjourned amid the cheers
and expressed determination of the Po
trero Republicans to work for the ticket.
AT GARIBALDI HALL.
Garibaldl Hall, on Broadway near Mont
gomery street, was crowded last night
with enthusiastic Republicans of the
Forty-fourth and Forty-fifth Assembly
Districts. George M. Perine acted as
chairman and introduced Colonel T. V.
Eddy as the first speaker. Colonel Eddy
ftaid that since the Supreme Court had de
cided in favor of the* new charter the
Republicans had shown a more deter
mined attitude and were now more con
fident of winning a fight that their oppo
-1 declared at the beginning of the
campaign was hopeless. As election day
approached this confidence asserted itself
in the ranks of the Republican party
Btronger and stronger. Horace Davis pos
sessed all the good traits of his polit
ical opponent and naught could be said
against his character. What he had done
9an Francisco and for California was
a matter of record. "Gentlemen," he
ided, "keep up this fee-ling of con
fldence; take off your c>ats and work
from now on, and on the Bth day of No
vember you will be proud to shake the
hand of our next Mayorâ Horace Davis."
The Knickerbocker quartet favored the
nee with some campaign selections,
and William A. Deane, Charles A. Low
William Watson and W. Z. Tiffany ad-
I the meeting.
Horace Davis then entered the hall and
was loudly cheered as he stepped to the
platform. Mr. Davis, In the course of
his remarks, said that it was his inten
tion, if the people saw fit to make him
Mayor of their city, to give them in re
turn a good, clean and honest adminis
tration.
"I am," he said, "in favor of a new era
for Baa Francisco. I want to see her
placed near the head of the list of fine
cities. A complete sewer system, new
â Ihouses, new hospitals and a fair
pi art toward the goal of commercial proa
pi ritj is what San Francisco is entitled
to. Mr. Davis urged his listeners to
keep up the good work and on election
day elect the Republican ticket from too
to bottom. v
THIRTIETH DISTRICT DEM
OCRATS.
The Democrats of the Thirtieth Assem
bly District met last evening in the San
P'rancisco Athletic Club rooms, at Sixth
and Shipley streets. A bonfire and a
brass band were a part of the attractions
The other attractions were the wander-
Ing candidates for office on the Demo
cratic ticket. P. J. Tomalty presided and
Introduced the speakers. Mayor Phelan
Major Geary L. F. Byingtbn, R p"
Doolan, Franklin K. Lane and half a
doz<=-n others were on the speaking lint
The principal speech of the evening was
made by Mayor Phelan. He said that the
Republicans of the city could not be
trusted because they were controlled by
corporations and they would serve the
corporations if they were elected to office
He hedged very strongly on this proposi
tion on second thought, and said that
there were Republicans who had voted
for him and he hoped that they would
discard national political principles and
vote for the Democratic ticket.

THE SA:N FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 25, 1899.
PLOT TO OPEN
THE INGLESIDE
RACE COURSE
Secret Planning of
Arch Schemers.
OUTLINE OF THE CONSPIRACY
GOLDEN PROMISES TO THE OUT
GOING SUPERVISORS.
Canvass of the Board Shows That the
Hopes of the Agitators
Are Without Foun
dation.
Ingleside's tragic record of disgrace,
defalcations and death was spread be
fore the world in The Call of Friday,
March 3, 1899. The history of the closing
of the track and the expulsion of the
horde that brought misery, degradation
and death to many households need not
be told again, but it is time to warn the
community that a conspiracy Is hatching
to provide ways and means for reopening
the course.
Of late there have been secret
' gatherings attended by men of the
j Rainey, Crimmins and Lanigan type,
who are plotting to restore the old order
lof things at insleslde. The schemers
believe that the public conscience is dulled
and public vigilance is relaxed. They
: fancy that men sitting in the Board of
i Supervisors may be persuaded or induced
] to view the suppression of racing at
Ingleside in a manner at variance with
1 former views. Corrigan, the "Master of
i Hawthorne," is here taking observations
i and giving the schemers the benefit of his
| advice. While the meetings have been
held in secret The Call has managed to
ascertain the motives and something of
the plans proposed by the conspirators.
The advocates of an open course, where
pool-selling and bookmaking shall be ulti
mately toleruted, do not propose to sweep
away all barriers at the iirst attack, but
hope to gain their ends by gradual ap
! proaches. Their present plan is to care-
I fully sound outgoing Supervisors to ascer
' tain how far they will dare go in opposi
! lion to the pronounced public sentiment
: against the old form of racing and gar
ni bllng at the track. The schemers beiieve
I that some of the Supervisors are about
I ready to weaken. Reports brought In by
: Crimmins, Rainey and Lajilgan may jua
! tify this belief in the secret circles of the
schemers.
An ordinance repealing the existing pro
hibition will not bo recommended, as the
leaders in the movement to reopen Ingle
side are convinced that such an ordinance
would he vetoed by Mayor Phelan. It has
| been suggested, therefore, that efforts be
directed to obtain support of a majority
I of the Board of Supervisors in favor of a
simple resolution allowing races to be
I conducted at the track. It is contended
that the Mayor's objection to such a
resolution would not defeat its purpose,
for tho reason that his signature would
not be r^-iuired to make the resolution ef
fective.
The Call is in possession of information
I obtained by a close canvass of the board,
; that a sufficient number of Supervisors
I cannot be tempted by the golden prom-
Isee of the conspirators and touts to
j change their views of the situation.
BURGLARS MAKE
A BOLD RAID
OF RESIDENCES
The Western Addition
Suffers.
A gang of burglars committed whole
sale burglaries in the fashionable pre
cincts of the Western Addition last night.
] Within two hours four residences were
broken into and looted, and in three cases
the thieves escaped with their booty and
I in each case they succeeded in getting
away without being seen. The police
were notified immediately by telephone to
the Central station, but before the detec
tives arrived the house-breakers were op
erating in other portions of the district.
They first broke into the residence of
: Mrs. S. 11. Proctor at 1917 Webster street.
, Mrs. Proctor was visiting friends, and the
; burglars forced the front door open with
| a "jimmy." They entered the bedrooms
; and carried off clothing, jewelry and other
valuables. The richest haul was made at
the residence of Attorney Barclay Henley,
2131 Green street.
Here they succeeded in carrying off al
most a sackful of valuable silverware
from the dining room, and jewelry, dla
! monds and other valuables from Mr.
: Henley's sleeping apartments. The burg
lars opened the front door with skeleton
; keys.
The lawyer was out at the time, and
it Is presumed that the house-breakers
had a lookout on the other side of the
street to notify them when the oppor
tunity to "touch off" the residence was
ripe. Henley returned shortly after the
burglars left and he immediately reported
the matter to police headquarters. While
the police were looking over Mr. Henley's
residence for a clew the daring thieves
were busily engaged plying their trade in
a residence at 2044 Jones street, where
they wore also successful in getting away
; with valuable jewelry.
Their last effort was at the home of E.
. A. Maxfleld, at 1314 Washington street,
I and here they met with defeat. Mrs.
; Maxfield had just retired when she was
i awakened by footstep* in the front yard.
By the time burglars opened a window
at the rear of the place she was moving
about with a light, thus frightening them
| off. The police visited Mrs. Maxfield's
house shortly after midnight hoping to
get a description of the men.

Hangman Lunt Improving.
Dr. J. Henry Barbat announced last
evening that Hangman Lunt of San Quen
tin Prison was rapidly regaining his
health of mind and body. The physician
said that his patient was suffering from
nervous exhaustion, with occasional spells
of melancholia, but with careful treat
ment and a much-needed rest, he would
soon be In a condition to go back to his
duties.
ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
AN OLD PIONEER HAS
GONE TO HIS REST
BERKELEY, Oct. 24.â One of California's oldest pioneers. James Mc-
Gee, passed away in the university town shortly before 3 o'clock
this afternoon.
Mr. McGee was 85 years of age and came to this State with the
early settlers of '49. A native of Ireland, he crossed to America
as a young man. He was early attracted to California, settling
upon his arrival in Alameda County. The old McGee homestead on Catha
rine street, between Dwlght way and Charming way, dates from the tlni3
of his first settlement here. In the early days the site upon which the
university town is now built was held by a quartet of large land owners,
of which Mr. McGee was one. The other three were F. K. Shattuck, Wil
liam Hillegasa and James Leonard. The four owned about equal tracts of
land. With the death of Mr. McGee the last of the four passes away. They
were all prominently identified with the progress and development of this
city and of this section of the county.
The estate left by the old pioneer consists chiefly of an extent of city
property known as the McGee tract. It comprises some of the most val
uable land in the city, extending over twenty-four blocks, and lying be
tween Addison street, Dwight way, California street and Grove street. A
portion of the tract was recently purchased by the Board of Trustees as
a site for the new City Hall.
Deceased leaves behind two daughters to mourn his lossâ Miss Mary Ellen
McGee and Miss Katherine McGee. The funeral will be held from the old
homestead next Friday afternoon.
DIRECTORS ARE
LOOKING FOR A
GOOD WARDEN
Robert McKillican in
View.
The State Prison Directors are carefully
looking over the field to find a Warden to
fill the vacancy tit the Folsom prison
caused by the death of Charles Aull.
Among the men considered was Thomas
Cunningham of Sail Joaquin. whose ex
tended and able services as Sheriff of that
county are recognized throughout the
State. The Directors, after mature delib
eration have reached the conclusion that
the new Warden at Folsom should be a
man in the prime of life, with an abun
dance of physical vitality and great per
sonal energy.
Recently a numbor of prominent men-
Democrats and Republicansâ held a con-
Cerence and decided to ask Robert Mc-
Killican of Alum-da County to become a
candidate. Thi: suggestion of Mr. McKll
lican's name was received with favor by
several of tho State Prison Directors, as
they recognized in him a man possessing
the qualifications that rendered the late
Ward* n Aull so efficient and acceptable.
When Mr. McKillican was sounded to as
certain if he would serve if elected ho
said to his friends that he was not a can
didate and was not seeking the office, yet
if the Prison Directors should regard him
as a man whose services were needed
and should offer him the position he
would accept the office.
It Is known that Mr. McKillican has the
support of many earnest Democrats and
Republicans. He made an excellent rec
ord as Sheriff of Alameda County, win
ning the confidence and respect of lead
ing men in Doth political parties. In the
political arena he is known as a straight
out Democrat.
The Prison Directors find it no easy task
to discover the material for a first-class
Warden. The man at the Immediate head
of prison affairs should possess a number
of Qualifications. He should be firm,
brave, vigilant, patient and just. Strength
of character should be united with vigor
of mind and body. Those about him and
around him are constantly on the alert
to detect a weak place in his make-up.
In many respects Charles Aull was the
ideal Warden. Men filling subordinate
places had respect for his judgment and
authority and convicts knew that he was
not a weakling. Mr. McKillican Is a
strong, even-tempered, well-balanced man
of sound judgment, resolute In purpose,
but withal patient and kind.

PRESIDENT WHEELER
INAUGURATED TO-DAY
BERKELEY, Oct. 24. -Benjamin Ide
Wheeler, formerly professor of Greek at
Cornell University, will be formally in
augurated as president of the University
of California to-morrow. In view of the
outlook which the institution has before
It of being architecturally one of the
largest in the country, as well as the
position held by President Wheeler In the
world of letters and education, the occa
sion will be one of the most significant
yet known In the university's history.
Arrangements for the ceremony are now
completed. The exercises will be held
within the cinder track on the college
campus, beginning at 2 o'clock. Carpen
ters have been at work for the last few
days building a platform within the in
closure and erecting extra seats for the
visitors. Aside from the regular trains
running from San Francisco to Berkeley
extra locals have been arranged for to
carry visitors every half hour between
11 and 2 o'clock.
Shortly before the time set for begin
ning a procession will be formed at Stiles
Hall, consisting of the members of the
Board of Regents, the faculty of the
university, together with the guests and
speakers of the afternoon. President
Wheeler will wear his doctor's robes, as
will also many of the other distinguished
collegians invited to be present. The
procession will march in academic state
from Stiles Hall to the cinder track in
closure, and their arrival there will be
the signal for the exercises to begin.
The programme will consist of speeches
by Regent Andrew S. Hallidio of the
Board of Regents, and by Presi
dent David Starr Jordan of Stan
ford University. President John Gll
man of Johns Hopkins Univer
sity, Baltimore, arrived from the East
last night. He will deliver the inaugural
address. President Wheeler will then
formally assume his office as president of
the university, and in his address is ex
pected to outline the policy which he ex
pects to pursue.
Should the weather prove unfavorable
the ceremony will be held in Harmon
gymnasium instead of upon the cinder
track, and the faculty procession from
Stiles Hall will be omitted.

CAHILLS COULD NOT
DISMISS DIVORCE SUIT
OAKLAND, Oct. 24.â After the scoring
she received at the hands of Superior
Judge Greene yesterday Mrs. Margaret
Cahill, who is being sued for divorce by
her husband. Christopher C. Cahill,
seemed more inclined to-day to settle mat
ters out of court.
When the matter of counsel fees and
costs came up again this morning It de
veloped that ihe parties had called at the
County Clerk's office, stating that they
desired the whole proceedings dismissed.
A blank had been given them, such as
is used for dismissals, and this they had
signed, but Judge Greene declined to rec
ognize the same as final, advising that
the dismissal could not very well be ac
complished without the consent of their
attorneys. The matter, therefore, was
once more continued until next Monday
to enable them to arrange matters with
their attorneys.
SHORT LIVED LOVE.
OAKLAND, Oct. 24.â Edward R. Per
rin of Alameda, who just one month ago
to-day led Mrs. Sarah Johnson to the
altar at San Leandro â¢as his bride, has
come to the s-ad realization that when age
meets age in matrimony then comes the
tug of war with more attending sorrows
than aged bachelorhood in all its loneli
ness.
When Perrin married Mrs. Johnson on
Sunday, September 24, he had just passed
his seventieth birthday anniversary, and
the ancient bride was but six years his
junior. He had previously occupied com
fortable apartments in an Alameda home,
while she owned a cozy cottage at San
Leandro, so that companionship was
rather inviting to them in their lonely
singleness, and many friends extended
congratulations upon the supposed good
fortune of the two souls meeting to enjoy
in comfort their declining years.
But when the aged groom abandoned
his bachelor quarters for the homestead
uf liis bride at San Leandro, peace and
happinrss soon flew away in opposite
directions, for with the responsibilities
that rest upon a husband's shoulders
came the necessary drag upon his purse
fof the purchase of many necessary
household articles and kitchen utensils,
and. If the st<sry now told by some of
their friends be true. Mr. Perrin found
that while he had looked upon matrimony
as an opportunity to save his dollars more
than formerly, Mrs. Perrin had looked
upon it in the same light from her side,
and further, if reports be true, he did not
ivlish the idea of spending his money and
his wife saving hers. A discussion of
finance? waxed Into a heated debate and
finally Pc-rrin tnok his clothes and left, ,
and now P^rrin resides in Oakland, while I
his bride of a month is ensconced in her
lonely cottage meditating over the next
step to take in her troubles.

UNIVERSITY QF CALIFORNIA.
BERKELEY. â â . ; .'.
NOTICE TO CONTRACTORS. BUILDERS
AND MATERIAL MEN.
Notice Is hereby given to all concerned that
sealed proposals or bids are solicited for the
erection of an addition to the Chemical Labo-
ratory building on the University grounds,
Berkeley, Cal., and for performing the labor
and furnishing the materials necessary there-
for: and said bids or proposals will be re-
ceived at the office of the Acting Secretary of
the Regents of the University, in South Hall,
Berkeley, Cal., at or before 3:30 o'clock p. m.,
of the 6tb day of November. 1899.
And notice is hereby further given that the
plans and specifications of said buildings have
been made and adopted and are placed- in the
office of Curlett & McCaw, architects, 314
Phelan building, San Francisco, where the
tame may be seen and examined during each
day, from 10 o'clock a. m. until 4 o'clock
p. m., from this date until day of '
No bids or proposals will be considered un-
less the same are accompanied by a certified
check or a bond of said proposer In the sum
of ten (10) per cent of his proposal, with suf-
ficient sureties, conditioned that, if said bid or
proposal shall be accepted, the party pro-
posing will duly enter Into a proper contract
to faithfully perform the duty and obligations
in accordance with said proposal, and plan
and specifications, which shall be and are
hereby required to be a part of such con-
tract. The right to reject any and all bids is
hereby reserved.
This notice is given pursuant. to an act, en-
titled "An act to amend an act entitled an
act to create and organize the University of
California," approved March 25, 1868, and an
act amendatory of section 25 thereof, approved
March 28, 1872, relating to the construction of
buildings, approved March 3, 1897.
By order of the Committee on Grounds and
Buildings of the Board of Regents.
E. A. DENICKE, Chairman.
"W. A. McKOWEN. Acting Secretary.
Dated October 24, 1899. ' â  â

MINERS BID GOOD-BY TO-DAY
California Association
Accomplishes Much
for the Industry in
Which It Is
Interested.
A New Board of Officers
Will Be Elected at
the Meeting to
Be Held This
Morning.
The California Miners' As
sociation decided yesterday
that the State should take up
thÂ« matter of water conserva
tion and not wait for national
aid.
Senator Perkins advised
the delegates that their fight
for a Secretary of Mines and
Mining was almost hopeless.
Reports of Secretary Ben
jamin and the various com
mittees showed the associa
tion to be in a most prosper
ous condition.
Last evening the delegates
from the Western States were
banqueted at the Palace.
To-day the association â will
elect a new board of officers.
In the afternoon the delegates
â¢will be the guests of the
Union Iron Works in an ex
cursion on the bay.
THE miners of California and the
visiting delegates from the States
west of the Kocky Mountains will
complete the business of their an
nual session to-day. The most im
ot matter which the convention
will dispose of will be the election of
a new board of officers, which was post
: from yesterday's session. The
reading and disposal of the'reports of the
various standing: committees occupied
much of the time of the convention's
Becond day. From the miners' point of
\ i.'\v, these reports wore of more than
Og interest, and they were instruc
tive as well to every Californian and, in
, every resident of the West.
ral able addresses were made,
among these being the remarks of Ben
ator George C. JVrkins, abounding as ]
.iid In reminiscences of the early
days of mining in the Golden State. i
The subject of the conservation of flood
: a difference of opinion ]
n the Miners' Association and the ' i
National Irrigation Congress, represented M
eorge H. Maxwell. Mr. Maxwell i
vehemently protested against the move- i
rd local action in the matter, 1
arguing that if the miners would be pa- <
tient the National Government would give I
the assistance nought, but that if the <
did not pull together the great na- s
lionai Irrigation agitation was ill danger of j
falling through. The convention was not 1
in harmony with Mr. Maxwell, and among j
the r> idopted was one in direct i
opposition t" his plea.

of yesterday's session promptly at the
appointed hour. The first announcement j
in invitation to the delegates from'
Henry T. and Irving M. Scott to visit the I
Vnion Iron Works and enjoy an excursion I i
on the bay as their guests. The offer was
accepted with thanks by the convention.

KING GEORGE OF
FUG ISLAND
DROPS IN AGAIN
Here on the Schooner
Eliza Miller.
Goorge Grelg, King of the Fanning
group, arrived from his capital on Wash
ington Island yesterday. He came up on
the schof ncr Eliza Miller, making the
voyage in thirty-two days, and reports
that very f< w changes have taken place
In his domain since he was last in San
Francisco. Washington is now the most
productive island of the group. The cocoa
nut trees on the other islands are not
bearing as well as they did five years ago
and new groves are being planted. There
has been very little sickness in the group
and the natives are contented and happy.
King Greig's son and heir was educated
in San Francisco, but he did not come up
with his father on this occasion. Next
. however, when the copra ship
comes to San Francisco, Prince Grelg
â¢will pay the city a visit. The Eliza Mll
, ler brought up IGS tons of copra and will
take back a supply of provisions for the
â Islanders.
The little Bchooner SadiÂ« arrived from
Kodlak yesterday after a passage of
tw< nty-flve days. She Is only four tons
burden, and yet two men navigated her
that 1750 miles of stormy sea and
reached the Gold'-ii Gate without a serious
mishap. Captain Lane says he and bis
companion ltft Kodiak on September 30.
For a wtek they had good weather and
then It began to bluw. The little schooner
Btood up to it well, however, and they
came out of the 6torm without any dam
age. Off the mouth of the Columbia
Kiver it blew a hurricane and the little
boat lay to a drag anchor without a Btltch
of canvas on her for twenty-four hours.
Captain Lane considers her as stanch a
little craft as ever was built, and says ha
would not be afraid to go around thÂ«
â world in her.
The Hawaiian sugar crop is nearly all
marketed now and many of the fleet are
returning to San Francisco in ballast.
The barkentlne S. G. Wilder came up in
i) days, the best run of the season.
The bark Annie Johnson came up in thir
teen and the barkentine Omega in six
days. The British bark Auldglrth
made the run from Iquique in sixty-two
days and the British ship William Tillie
from Newcastle, Australia, in seventy
eix days. There were sixteen arrivals yes
terday and all of them had fair weather
passages. The Umatllla was a few hours
late, owing to a strong head wind that
lasted all the way from Flattery to San
Francisco.
The Occidental and Oriental Steamship
Company's Gaelic sailed for Hongkong
via Honolulu and Yokohama yesterday.
She took away every passenger she could
.iccommodate and every pound of freight
fihe could carry. There were 98 cabin
passengers, 666 Chinese and 28 Japanese
In the steerage and 39 Chinese who werÂ©
deported by the courts. The exodus of
<"hlnese is due to the desire of the Mon
golians to spend the new year holidays at
home. Among the cabin passengers waa
Bishop Potter of New York, who in going
to Honolulu and thence to Manila on a
vielt. Mrs. &. T. Osborne. wife of the Pa
cific Mall Company* agent at Yokohama,
were but twenty-one hungry miners in
the convention. The Judge was astounded
at the result, but he was equal to the
emergency, and in a ringing speech
turned the tide in favor of the feed, and
had nearly every delegate in the hall
shouting for the spread before he had
finished.
"Shame on California," he said, "if she
cannot keep up her reputation for liber
ality and hospitality at this time and en
tertain these guests of ours as they de
serve as the representatives of the great
est industry of our nation. Boys, get up
on your hind legs and attend this banquet
to-night!" And the "boys" stood up,
every mother's son of them.
Following this President Neff called
Senator Perkins to the platform. As the
gentleman arose from the body of the
assemblage and started for the stage he
was greeted by a mighty cheer. He said:
"I did not come here to talk but to
greet my old friends. Like most of you,
I have spent many of my days polishing a
drill or running a tunnel to a vein that
pitched the wrong way." He eulogized
the miners, stating that through their
work the credit of the country had been
placed on a footing equal to that of any
nation of the world. He congratulated
Ithe association for the good work it had
done In the past eight years In benefiting
and developing the great industry in
which its members were interested. j
Speaking of the miners' determination to
have created the Secretaryship of Mines
and Mining, he said:
"It is all right for you to fly your kites
high, but I do not think there Is any prob
ability of such a position being created
for many years to come. You may secure
a Commissioner of Mines and Mining,
which will bring about the very object in
view. I speak advisedly. The commer
cial interests of the nation have been try
ing for fifteen years to get a Secretary of
Commerce, without success. There are
already too many heads in the Cabinet. I
I hope this convention will take a prac
tical view of the situation and take what
it can get. "With you I rejoice in Califor
nia's resources. No one loves to sing her
praises better than I, and no one takes
more glory in it; but we must remember
i that there are other great States. The
coal and Iron industries demand recog
i nition, and we must work with them if
I we expect to accomplish anything in the
direction indicated. "We cannot be a com
munity within ourselves.
1 "If judge Searls and I had our way, the
miners of the State would have every
thing they asked for. They deserve noth
ing but prosperity and happiness, and I
would like to see them go down the In
cline of their lives on a pathway strewn
with roses. I never forget that I am your
servant, and all that I have or expect to
tret I owe to the good people of Cali
fornia."

was also a passenger. Mrs. Osborne is
the daughter of the late Captain Shot
well, managing director of the Merchants'
1 Exchange. As the Gaelic passed out
through the heads John Hyslop, the ex
, change reporter at Point IÂ»bos, dipped
I his signal flags in honor of Mrs. Osborne,
! and the Gaelic acknowledged the salute.
First Officer C. P. Jones of the Gaelic
; has remained ashore this voyage and will
take a well-earned vacation. The other
j officers were promoted a step for the voy
age, Second Officer Burrows going out as
I first officer and Third Officer Kilbury as
second officer.
Several of the transports moved from
one wharf to another yesterday, hut none
of thorn went to sea. The Olymiiia went
out into the stream, but as the repairs to
hÂ»r boilers are not completed it may be
this evening before she gets away. The
Senator was moved to Broadway wharf,

Eugene Aubert fractured his leg In two
places in a very simple manner yester
day. He was walking along Sutter street
when he slipped on a banana peel and fell.
He was taken to the Harbor Hospital,
where Dr. Robinson reduced the fracture.

FOOTE AND CHURCH
COLLIDE IN COURT
CHARGES OF BLACKMAILING
AND COWARDICE.
Warm Morning Session With Oppos
ing Attorneys in Bellicose At
titudes Makes the Air
Smell of Powder.
Evidence in the sensational Hite divorce
case, conducted before Judge Jones of
Martinez, in the offices of Van R. Pater
son, was as nothing compared with the
bellicose demonstrations of the interested
attorneys in the trial of the case yester
day morning and afternoon. Every vari
ety of bad blood was shown and the talk
was all of blackmail, cowardice, pistols
fr>r two or three and blood-stained fields,
where honor's battles have been fought
to the mortal finish.
William S. Church, one of the attorneys
for the Indian woman, was on one side of
the lingual war and W. W. Foote, who
Delegates Insist That

at Washing^

accomplished fact and the two sections,
proud in the protection afforded to each
by a generous government, will, aiding
each other, reach that prosperity which
their unparalleled resources unfettered
will bring te all."
J. M. Gleavea spoke on the subject of
the conservation of flood waters. He ar
gued that the State should issue bonds
for the construction of the necessary res
ervoirs. He was strongly opposed to
waiting for national aid. "The Lord helps
him who helps himself." he said, "and if
we do not do something in this important
matter there is little probability of any
thing being done."
This called forth a hot retort from
George H. Maxwell, representing the Na
tional Irrigation Congress. He advised
the convention to proreed with the great
est care and not to make any mistake in
the matter. He did not believe it was
possible for anything to be accomplished
in the line Indicated without the aid of
the National Government. "The people
who need water for Irrigation and mining
should Join hands with those who want
protection from floods," he said. "Do not
go by yourselves and throw cold water
on this great national undertaking."
An interesting paper on petroleum min
| Ing was read by A. H. Ricketts. At to
' day's meeting he will introduce a resolu-
I tion asking Congress to give the same
rights to those prospecting for petroleum
as Is now accorded lode miners in running
their tunnels.
Resolutions were adopted indorsing all
of the recommendations made by the va
rious committees: pledging unfaltering
support to the Caminettl act; approving
the plans of the Governor of Colorado for
a miners' convention to take up the mat
i ter of a Secretary of Mines and Mining;
asking Congress to open Pyramid Lake
and Walker Lake, Indian reservations,
Nevada, situated in the mineral regions;
I Indorsing a uniform system of mining
) laws rules and regulations throughout
! the United States.

represents Millionaire Hite, was on the
other. Hardly had Judge Jones called the
morning session when Attorney Church
arose with his grievance engraved upon
his countenance. On Thursday last, in
the absence of Church, Attorney Foote
made charges of attempted blackmail
against Church. For three days the lat
ter has been nursing his wrath and it
burst on the courtroom yesterday morn
ing and nearly resulted in some sort of
a mixup.
Church's denunciation of Foote was
bitter, insinuating that he was born on
the "dark and bloody ground" and would
not lie dormant when his honor was as
sailed. John A. "Wall, another attorney
representing the Indian woman, had also
been singed by Foote's charges and he
entered his throaty forces in the charge
against the common enemy. From time
to time the court was called upon to force
a speedly conclusion of the war-talk, but
the tide could not be stemmed. Finally
W. W. Foote arose and half scornfully
half seriously threatened to exterminate
the entire opposition. He said he Was
ready for them all or one at a time,
whichever they preferred and whichever
way they wished to take their medicine
The strained relations continued
throughout the day. During the after
noon session, while Attorney Hanlon was
examining John R. Hite, Foote objected
to Hanlon's method of questioning the
witness. One word led to another, "Foote
concluding by softly remarking to Han
lon that there was enough bull-baiting
going on in the trial and that it had better
be discontinued for the good of all or else
something would happen. Later on Hanlon
asked a question of the witness to which
Foote objected. Hanlon then arose and
excitedly cried out that it was the most
important thing in his life â the day on
which the marriage license was obtained.
Foote replied that if the case continued
there would be some more important
things "in the life of others of us," re
ferring to himself, Hanlon and Van R.
Paterson. Again, the suggestion of
powder and guns was in the air.
Several important witnesses were put
on the stand yesterday. In the afternoon
John R. Hite was called by the plaintiff,
who attempted to show that the license
for the defendant to wed Cecilia Nouges
was taken out October 11 or 12, 1597, and
on the 13th, when the marriage was con
summated, Hite was not in a condition
to know what he was doing. Much time
was consumed in eliciting from Hite just
what times he was present at the Indian
Peak Ranch, but nothing positive was ob
tained except the acknowledgment of a
visit in 1893.
The witness who furnished the comedy
part of the melodrama was John Fergu
non, the keeper of a roadhouse near Hite's
Cove, some thirteen miles from Mariposa.
He lived in that part of the country,
knew Hite, the Indian woman and re
ferred to her as Mrs. Hite, but in cross
examination he admitted that she was
known by a number of men's names. The
attorneys for the plaintiffs tried to prove
that Ferguson knew of the relations be
tween Hite and the so-called Mrs. Hite.
Arthur Rodgers was put on the stand
and he told of a visit paid him by F. \V.
Berlin with an offer to compromise the
case. Berlin offered $13,000, but Rodgers
declared that $30,000 was the minimum fig
ure, with an acknowledgment of wifehood
for his client. He also admitted that
money had been advanced by himself
and Attorney Hanlon for the prosecution
of the case. The action will be taken up
at 10 o'clock to-day.
Bouton Must Stay in Jail.
George P. Curtis applied yesterday to
the United States District Court for a
â¢writ of habeas corpus on behalf of Victor
Bouton, the petition alleging that Bouton
is restrained of his liberty by Chief of
Police Lees, in defiance of law, under a
warrant for grand larceny issued by a
Justice of the Peace of Humboldt County,
Nevada.
Judge de Haven refused to issue the
writ because it did not appear from the
petition that the imprisonment of Bouton
was in violation of the constitution or of
any law or treaty of the United States.

MURDERER WINTERS
TEMPORARILY SAVED
WARDEN ATJXL'S DEATH SUS
PENDS THE EXECUTION.
Harry Winters, under sentence of death,
will have a few more days on earth be
fore his neck is submitted to the noose.
He was to have been executed on next
Friday at the State prison at Folsom, but
the recent death of Warden Aull has de
layed that event.
It will be remembered that on the night
of November 17, 1&97, Winters and a fel
low named C. H. Reynolds entered the
Grand Hotel at South San Francisco, San
Mateo County, and attempted to rob the
landlord and guests. There was a sharp
I fight in which a number of shots were
fired by the robbers and one bullet struck
Charles A. Andrews with fatal results.
The murderer escaped and was not cap
tured for a number of days. In time he
was sentenced to pay the death penalty
â  for hid crime and Warden Aull was the
| State official who was directed to execute
I the sentence.
Soon after Mr. Aull's death the Prison
I Directors met and failed to select a sue
| cessor to the late Warden. However, they
j appointed R. J. Murphy aa deputy
Warden with powers to attend to the
business and duties of the Warden. Mur
phy was not sure aa to whether this au
thority empowered him to execute the
sentence of death in Winters' case, and as
the time set is next Friday he requested
an opinion of the Attorney General. Mr.
j Ford, after reviewing the statutes on such

matters, decided that Murphy Is not
under the law authorized to carry out the

Y\ hile in the usual run of business the
acting Warden may transact the business
of the higher officer, it would be unsafe
to do so under the circumstances
Attorney General Ford suggests two
courses that the deputy Warden may pur
sue. One is that the prisoner may be re
moved to the court and resentenced, the
execution to be held in San Quentln
Prison by the Warden of that institution.
The other is that the Governor may be
induced to reprieve the prisoner until
some certain date, and in the meantime
the Prison Directors would appoint a
regular Warden. In any event Winters
will be allowed to live a few days longer.
